1.1 Objectives   
The last decade has experienced 
a strong increase in technological 
developments for improved 
material functionality. One of these 
involves surface treatments, which 
employ complex (multilayer) 
structures designed to provide 
multifunctionality in a single 
solution. Within this proposal text, 
for the sake of simplicity, we will 
refer to these  complex 
 multilayer  surface treatments 
as ‘coatings’. This definition 
includes functional and 
performance coatings on 
textiles, paints on plastics, foil 
lamination and adhesion layers 
for plastics  parts  (polymer-
polymer  and polymer-metal 
 connections).  These ‘coatings’

 have  a  wide  range  of advantages and benefits but currently cannot be recycled because of 
their hybrid nature.   
The main goal of DECOAT is to enable the circular use of coated parts by  
developing novel smart polymer material systems, and their corresponding recycling processes , 
currently a missing link – see figure. We will demonstrate the project results via concrete use-cases 
for outdoor gear, household electronics and automotive parts . A systemic approach for delivering 
breakthroughs all along the value chain will be followed, see figure.   
To reach this ambitious goal and to maximise the impact of our DECOAT project on the textile and 
plastics sector, we define the following concrete key objectives (KO):  
• KO1. Development of a toolbox of triggerable materials for debonding on demand suitable for 
use on textiles and plastics. Result: ‘coatings’1 that can be removed by various triggers, e.g. ther mal 
via infra-red, heat or steam (targeted temperatures 100-150°C), chemical treatment or ‘microwaves’2.  
• KO2. Development and design of triggerable debonding coating solutions  for ‘disassembly’ of 
parts or objects and for ‘removal’ of the coating from the  multilayer structure. Focus is on acrylates, 
polyurethanes and PVC materials. Result: generic disassembly and removal solutions for punctual 
connections (0D), linear joints (1D) and planar coatings (2D).  
• KO3. Automation of sorting according to the required trigger. Result: pilot line enabling to sort the 
coated textiles and plastics based on the separation trigger incorporated.   
• KO4. Integration of debonding systems in a continuous pilot plant recycling line for coated 
parts. Result: pilot line enabling the recyclability of coated parts (flexible and rigid). The system will 
integrate the following elements: sorting and grinding, the novel debonding system, mechanical 
recycling and melt extrusion, all steps running in a continuous way.  
• KO5. Demonstration via use cases (outdoor gear, household electronics and automotive parts) using 
the pilot recycling plant and the adapted coating processes during manufacturing. Result: circularity 
demo of the use case products. Technology and processing for the use cases brought to TRL  5, 
accompanied by a plan for upscaling & industrialisation.   
• KO6. Assessment of environmental impact and implementation of safety -by-design (safe 
product, safe production and safe use and recycling). Result: full LCA study for the debonding 
concepts; reduced uncertainties and risks for human and environmental safety of DECOAT solutions.   
• KO7. Exploitation and business plans  for the DECOAT res ults, including for the use cases 
manufacturing guidelines to ensure implementation at industrial scale. Further, for the more generic 
results (design-material concepts) a thorough analysis will be done for wide scope exploitation within 
the textile and plastics sectors. Result: tailored business plans for DECOAT (company) partners, public 
research roadmap and a strategy to further exploit and develop the generic DECOAT results, as a 
basis for implementing the market creating potential of the DECOAT approach.   
• KO8. Dissemination and communication among relevant European communities. The main 
target is the European materials and recycling community, supplemented by the wider stakeholders 
group, both for getting extra feedback during project implementation an d for promoting our results.  
Result: raise awareness,  engage for collaboration and adaptation/integration of the project 
developments in applications beyond those covered within DECOAT.
1.2 Relation to the NMBP work plan  
The DECOAT products will be designed to enable separation (and thus recycling) by incorporating smart 
additives in the adhesive that are activated by a specific trigger (e.g. heat, humidity, microwave, chemical, 
mechanical). Additionally, an efficient sorting process correlated with the trigger and a pilot recycling plant 
will be developed to illustrate circular use. In this fashion, DECOAT will directly respond to two of the

1.3 Concept and approach  
(a) Overall concept underpinning DECOAT  
The playing field. Increasing recycling is crucial, especially for plastics and textiles, as illustrated by 
the recently published European strategy on plastics 5 or the voluntary engagement of the plastics 
industry to recycle 50% by 20406. However large the need and however impressive the targets, today 
only 6% of new plastic materials used stems from recycling 7. Also within the textile industry the use 
of recycled material is limited: for clothing only about 1% is closed loop, ca 12% cascade recycling 3. A 
great deal of work is underway to improve this (al so via European projects), focussing on technical, 
economic and logistical aspects. As a result, we assume in the near future well-established networks 
throughout Europe where waste is collected and brought to local recycling facilities . At first, this 
network will deal with the current generation of products and materials that are not made for recycling. 
Consequently, only a small fraction of all materials is being recycled because the cost to the yield and 
quality ratio is too high compared with the one of virgin materials. Therefore, the next steps are products 
designed for recycling and made of intrinsically recyclable materials . This concept is being 
developed, e.g. for high added value applications like aerospace. However, for vast application areas this 
principle is not very advanced yet.   
The identified need. DECOAT will implement a solution for a specific class of hard to recycle items: 
textiles and plastics parts with (multilayer) ‘coatings’ . For plastics, these can be found eg in 
automotive, construction and electronics consumer goods. For textiles, typical uses are for technical 
textiles (outdoor gear, protective clothing, automotive applications and construction) and interior textiles 
(e.g. upholstery, mattress covers or curtains). The coating can be added for performance or aesthetics 
reasons. Coating, paints or adhesives have to possess excellent adhesion because of the durability of the 
coated product, and as such cannot easily be removed. Consequently, full objects are discarded, even 
though the coating often only comprises some % of the total weight . With DECOAT we will reduce 
the amount of waste material to the specific adhesive. 
This holds for end-of-life products but it is also one of 
the types of production scraps that are not efficiently 
recycled yet. Within this larger topic of debonding an 
emerging specific challenge is how to deal with  
scrap and end-of-life parts containing integrated 
electronics (from smart textiles, smart plastics and 
wearables).   
DECOAT proposed solution: enable the recycling 
of coated parts, which refers to a two -level 
approach: (i) ‘disassembly’ is separating the 
connections among the different physical parts; (ii) 
‘debonding’ or ‘removal’ refers to the removal of 
coatings or tie layers from the fabric or plastics object. 
This 2-step approach is illustrated in the figures for  a 
backpack. These two steps can be performed 
separately (or simultaneously in some cases) during the recycling process. DECOAT triggerable 
separations mechanisms will be suited for both steps and fill the missing gap for circular use of coated 
parts (see sketch in section 1.1). Within DECOAT, we will focus mostly on the 2nd level, i.e. the removal of 
coatings. But, the same principle of triggered debonding enables the disassembly step. So, DECOAT 
developments are relevant for both steps and we will refer to the processes as ‘debonding’.   
Interdisciplinary aspects : DECOAT combines expertise and knowledge from several disciplines: 
polymer chemistry, physics, coatings and paints, textiles and plastics processing, recycling, 
design, and environmental science . This is needed and allows tackling the interdisciplinary issues

materials to industrial requirements for coated textiles and plastics.   
Stakeholder involvement: interaction with the stakeholders is deemed crucial, especially with the 
waste actors. Therefore, ISWA has been added as partner , a worldwide association of solid waste 
actors that promote sustainable waste management and perform dissemination and  policy work. For 
further strong interaction with a wide group of stakeholders, the DECOAT Advisory and Support Group 
(ASG) has been established, containing 17 members from coating and recycling companies, industry 
associations, a public authority, standar disation bodies and a publisher, see also section 3.2.1.1f and 
section 4 for a detailed description.  
Societal engagement: DECOAT results will create a new market of recycling coated textiles and 
plastics, which will lead to job creation. Of special intere st is (partial) disassembly which enables repair, 
e.g. in small local workshops.

in the table below:  
Technology element – assessment of status  Start  Target  
Microwaveable (MW) additives  exist but manufacturing and design methodologies 
need to be developed for integration in coatings and paints for the DECOAT 
applications. Rely on triggering of MW additives for fast curing thermoset resins 
and polyester annealing, which is well-established.  
3  5  
Microcapsules for debonding coatings: concepts based on heat, steam, microwaves are 
shown at lab scale (CTB and NTUA). R&D needed to validate debonding in relevant 
environments.  
3  5  
Bonding/debonding adhesives: industrially available (e.g. INDAR) for applications with 
metals and ceramics but needs to be transferred to polymeric/textile materials  
4  5-6  
CreaSolv® process was developed for recycling of soluble thermoplastics. It will be 
applied to dissolve polymeric coatings from textiles and polymer surfaces. After 
separation, the coating polymer can be recovered from the solvent. Suitable 
solvents need to be developed for specific coating chemistry.  
3  5  
Trigger assisted mechanical recycling : mechanical recycling as established in paper 
industry will be modified to treat plastic parts and textiles that contain coatings 
and/or paints by incorporating additional elements (debonding system & sorting 
technology) and redesign other ones (grinding & drying system)  
4  5-6  
Automatic sorting technology: is used in the plastic recycling sector, based on a 
number of monitoring technologies. The right technology is to be selected and 
calibrated for novel DECOAT triggerable materials.  
3  5  
Summarized: we start from technologies with TRL 3 (-4) and target TRL5 (-6) by the end of DECOAT. 
As much as possible conventional processing technologies will be used, which eases scaling up. We 
will continuously monitor the performance of the technologies along the project to ensure reaching TRL 5, 
as first steps towards future commercialization. Linked (inter)national research and innovation 
actions.

(b) Overall Methodology:   
For describing the general DECOAT methodology, we group the DECOAT activities according to lab-scale, 
pilot scale and demonstration. The supporting activities are mentioned separately.  
Materials and material structures R&D at lab scale   
‘Triggerable materials’ : R&D on the  microcapsules, nano -additives, their integration in coatings, 
characterisation of their effects when triggered. We will develop a portfolio of techniques, each will be 
characterised, listing the strengths and weaknesses for the respective application domains. Integration in 
a coating system, directly in the coating or via a primer.  Triggerable debonding coating solutions both 
for the ‘disassembly’ of parts 
(‘1st level) and for the ‘removal’ 
of the coating from the 
substrate (2nd level).   
Process development at 
pilot scale:  Sorting 
technology: Research on 
sorting to discriminate post-
consumer and post production 
waste based on type of 
debonding trigger to ensure 
the materials can be identified 
& guided  to  the 
 fitting  recycling technique.  
Piloting of a recycling line 
for coated parts: conception, 
design and building of an 
actual pilot line, performing 
test runs on generic coated 
textiles and plastic parts, 
validation via the recycling of 
the use cases prototypes.  
Demonstration of circularity 
via use cases (see sketch): 
The novel coatings will be 
applied in the existing processes by the manufacturers/ endusers via three use cases. The (small scale) 
production of products will undergo the recycling process, the recovered  materials  will  be 
characterised and re-used. Evaluation of the reprocessed products.  
  
Transversal activities:  
Environmental & safety assessment : detailed assessment of environmental (full LCA) aspects and 
development of an ecodesign tool. Ensure  Safety-by-Design implementation via use of non -hazardous 
substances, e.g. for microcapsules or solvents (EC Regulation No. 1272/2008). Apply proper recycling 
and waste treatment (Directive 2008/98/EC).   
Innovation management: Economic assessment (full cost of ownership, business plans); dissemination: 
raise awareness from early on to maximise wide uptake of DECOAT results; communication to promote 
public awareness of circular economy values; focus on interaction with wider stakeholders and with the 
ASG.   
Coordination: efficient project implementation, project risk management.

Gender analysis. We will follow gender driven innovation and develop gender neutral demonstrators.

1.4 Ambition  
DECOAT aims at overcoming obstacles from current recycling technologies by designing coating 
formulations with smart additives, which allow adequate sorting, separation and recycling . 
Developments of separation and recycling technologies will be investigated and new separation 
technologies utilising novel mechanical and chemical recycling procedures will be validated, to meet the 
EU's circular economy and environmental targets.  
1.4.1  Progress beyond state-of-
the-art  
Building further on the available 
knowledge, expertise and patented IP 
within the DECOAT consortium, we 
will research and develop a 
debonding system based on 
triggerable additives, mechanical 
recycling, chemical recycling 
(Creasolv process) and latest coating 
(monitoring) technology. We will investigate two debonding systems: (i) a single layer with the trigger 
inside and (ii) a two -layer system consisting of a 
standard coating and a ‘primer’ layer (see 
sketches). Note that ‘primer’ is used here as 
common name for tie layer or primer or base layer. 
For selected combinations of the coating-substrate 
materials, we will research several triggers and 
several options for the actual removal of the 
coating (process conditions, 1-step versus 2-step). 
The output will  be a portfolio of debonding 
strategies, mentioning for each of the solutions the 
pros and cons. This novel information will form the basis for our DECOAT demonstration of circular use 
of coated parts but also be made available to the wider stakeholder community for further use.   
In the next paragraphs we describe the concrete progress beyond the state -of-the-art that DECOAT 
ambitions for (i) triggerable materials, (ii) optical monitoring for process optimisation and sorting 
of the coated waste, (iii) actual recycling and (iv) eco-design aspects.  
(i) Triggerable materials and related debonding systems – mainly related to WP1 & WP2 work  
Coatings that debond on demand  lose their adhesive function when an external trigger is applied. 
Partner CTB researched a novel separation layer for textiles by embedding thermally expandable 
syntactic microspheres (in FP7 proje ct ECOMETEX). Separation concept was shown on lab scale in 
boiling water, which is not an efficient process as it needs extensive energy (heating, drying). Partner 
RESCOLL developed an  innovative, patented (see section 1.4.2) interfacial debonding adhesive  
technology (INDAR™ Inside ) permitting the dismantling of structural aerospace assemblies without 
damage and was used for selective thermal stripping of aircraft paints. Partner NTUA has developed a 
variety of ‘smart’ nano- and microcapsules based on various materials (ceramics, metals, polymers), 
that react on external stimuli (water, mechanical stresses, microorganisms, etc), in projects LORCENCIS 
and BYEFOULING (see also project table above). Partner CTECH developed additives for interaction 
with microwaves. Based on that, a unique patented (polyester) yarn was developed, which disintegrates 
during microwaving11. This yarn enables easy and inexpensive removal of e.g. zippers (disassembly step). 
                                                
9 Source: http://www.vaude.com/angebote/nachhaltigkeitsbericht/2013_Nachhaltigkeitsbericht_screen_ENGL.pdf

DECOAT novelty: Using the available IP and expertise, we will  develop triggerable additives for water-
based coating formulations with a lowered debonding temperature as compared to existing procedures, 
although exceeding the processing and working temperature required/set by industrial demo cases. A 
sufficient difference between the manufacturing, maximal service and the debonding temperature will be 
ensured and will be realised by improving the heat direction towards the additives, e.g. by heat focussing 
layers or thermal conductive paints. Thermo -responsive core -shell nanostructured copolymers, Super 
Absorbent Polymers (SAP) and novel hybrid microwave susceptible materials (based on metal 
nanoparticles, silicon carbides and multiwall carbon nanotubes MWCNTs) will be investigated. Separation 
will be done by heat sources (like steam, microwaves and IR) that are available at industrial level to avoid 
the need for additional, or only very limited, drying. To reach a clean surface after debonding, we focus on 
controlled interfacial decohesion. For detection purposes, fluo rescent molecules will be added on the 
“smart” additives (dyes).  
The table below gives an overview of the materials to be developed and the polymer coating 
matrices to which they can be added. Already a first matching between the trigger mechanism and 
the polymer matrix is made, based on the chemistry of the particles and what is available in literature. 
As mentioned this is a first matching, further investigation within the DECOAT project is needed. Below 
the table, each of the triggerable materials is di scussed, giving more info on their synthesis and the 
challenges for their use in an actual debonding system.

Thermo-responsive core-shell nanostructured co-polymers  
Partner NTUA has vast experience in synthesis and development of tailored microcapsules and 
nanoadditives with functional properties (currently at TRL3)19,20,21. Partner DEVAN has built over the 
years a strong IP portfolio of microcapsules for various applications22.  
DECOAT novelty: we will make progress on the following topics to realise this triggerable material:  
Microcapsule core: selection of polymers with thermo -responsive properties or inclusion of a ctive 
ingredients that help delamination of textile layers (eg. poly(propyl methacrylate) or poly(butyl 
methacrylate).

Smart plastic materials with intrinsic recycling properties by design  
Research & Innovation Action addressing CE-NMBP-26-2018  
  
[DECOAT]    Page 14 of 70  
This proposal version was submitted by GUY BUYLE on 28/06/2018 16:55:43 Brussels Local Time. Issued by the Participant Portal Submission Service. 
Microcapsule shell: selection of suitable shell components to enclose the core material. In thermo-
responsive core-shell nanostructured co-polymers, a protective thermoplastic shell with tunable 
glass transition temperature (Tg) and melting temperature (Tm), will be tailored to have a higher Tg 
than the core’s polymer. The Tg is highly related to segmental polymer chain motion. As polymers warm 
up, the shell with the high Tg will act as a protective coating for the polymeric core, which will be in melting 
conditions at elevated temperature. When the ambient temperature surpasses the Tg of the shell, the inner 
core (material) will have a high chain mobility enabling detaching of the surfaces. At this defined moment 
the core material is released from the capsule, triggering de bonding. On the one hand, the shells should 
not react with the core material and on the other hand they should have a good chemical compatibility with 
the surrounding matrices. Examples of possible materials are: melamine resins, polyurea/polyurethanes, 
polyacrylates. In addition, the shells should provide a good storage for the active ingredients during the 
entire lifetime of the products, and only rupture/or release the active ingredient when triggered (end of the 
product life).  
Functionalization of the protective thermoplastic shell to increase its compatibility with a wide 
range of ‘coating’ formulations, also for INDAR technology (see below). The main materials: PU, PVC, 
acrylic, epoxies. Of potential further interest: amino, cyanates, isocyanides, hyd roxyls, sulphonamides, 
and phthalates. DECOAT consortium is aware that some forms of these substances have restrictions, so 
REACH compliance will be checked (see also Task 6.1).  
The use of the microcapsules in the debonding system : The microcapsule concentration (ca. 5% or 
less) and cross-linking density of the adhesive will be tailored and optimized, for strong adhesion during 
product use but also for ease of debonding at end-of-life. The latter will be achieved by exposure to heat 
as this will melt the shell, and thus activate the core. Homogeneous heat dissipation and activation of the 
microcapsules will be obtained by adding superparamagnetic nanoparticles (Fe 3O4). Application of 
irradiation will locally (=near the microcapsules) raise the 
temperature10,11. The aim is to develop materials that will be 
able to create ‘soft flexible points’ inside the crosslinked 
adhesive network in the presence of the external stimuli, 
thus improving detachment.  
Super Absorbent Polymers (SAPs)   
SAPs can absorb and retain extremely large amounts of a liquid 
relative to their own mass. Water-absorbing polymers, which are 
classified as hydrogels when cross -linked12, absorb aqueous 
solutions through hydrogen bonding with water molecules. In deionized and distilled water, a SAP may 
absorb 300 times its weight (from 30 to 60 times its own volume) and can become up to 99.9% liquid.  
DECOAT Novelty: The SAP materials  will exhibit a spherical shape with a two-layered structure: the 
polymeric core will be a lightly crosslinked hydrogel and the outer surface will be a highly crosslinked layer. 
The water absorption capacity of the SAPs is influenced by the nature and relative amounts of monomers 
used in the synthesis13. In this sense, the regulation of the crosslinker amount (both in the core and shell 
material) will be the key-property to achieve the desired degree of water absorbency for the debonding 
effect as well as the required protection of the hydrophilic core for obtaining a stable waterborne 
formulation14. Clearly, the amount of crosslinker is a decisive parameter for the swelling behaviour of

hydrogels and the aim is to achieve a compromise between the crosslinking level and a stable gel 
structure15,16.  
The effect of the pH on the water absorbency of 
the SAP spheres will be also investigated. The 
dispersion of the coreshell SAP materials will be 
investigated in an aqueous solvent where swelling 
equilibrium experiments will be performed to study 
water absorption at different pHs. When the pH 
favours the re pulsive forces between the 
polymeric chains, the water absorbency is 
increased. On the other hand, when a screening 
effect of the polar groups of the polymer chains takes place, the repulsive forces between the charged 
polymer chains are minimized and the water absorbency in greatly decreased. Based on this 
phenomenon26, the pH value of the waterborne formulations developed will be selected based on the value 
where the minimum water absorbency is observed. In this way, the SAP materials will be in a not swollen 
state during their incorporation in the coatings and they will be activated only upon the trigger.  
To apply the MW trigger, these structures will be also functionalized with  thermally-responsive 
magnetic nanoparticles (eg Mn-Zn ferrites or Ni or iron oxide) which will be selected based on the Curie 
temperature that matches the crosslinked layer’s melting point17. This functionalization will make the shell 
more susceptible to heating so that the shell can be selectively molten, as described also for the  case of 
the thermo-responsive core-shell co-polymers, allowing the lightly crosslinked hydrophilic core to absorb 
a large amount of water, causing the swelling and activating the debonding.  
To apply the Creasolv process,  lipophilic super-absorbent polymers18 for the absorption of the 
desirable organic solvents will be prepared by selecting the appropriate compounds both for the polymer 
chains that control the compatibility with the media and the ionic groups that are dissociable in polymer 
gels (e.g. alkyl ammonium salts). Candidate materials include acrylate monomers copolymerized with 
methacrylates as crosslinkers to yield lipophilic polyelectrolyte gels. The nature of the organic solvent and 
specifically its polarity (dielectric constant) will influence t he swelling behaviour of the SAP materials 
produced. For this reason, the ionic group content will be adjusted to avoid the formation of physical 
interactions which will negatively influence the swelling behaviour of the lipophilic SAP.   
Hybrid materials reacting to MW triggers  
Two forms of dielectric heating are commonly used industrially differing in wavelength of the 
applied electromagnetic radiation. These are Rf heating using wavelengths at 27 and 40MHz and 
MW heating using 2.45Ghz and 896MHz . Depend ing upon the material to be processed, there are 
advantages and disadvantages of using the different forms of radiation, but the main advantage is the 
direct heating of the material by the application of the radiation with no external heat transfer or hot surface 
required. Using Mw and Rf can be done either as a batch system or a semi batch system. In the latter 
case the materials are placed into a screened cavity and then heated by the application of the desired 
power for a defined time. This gives some advantages in the design because the system can be enclosed 
ensuring no leakage of radiation. Materials can be introduced in containers, treated and then removed to 
be processed further. Continuous processes are also possible for both Rf and MW systems for e xample 
conveyers can be used to introduce and remove the materials into the applicator zone.    
The main microwave absorption mechanism of a polymer is reorientation of the dipoles  when 
submitted to an electric field, the presence of these groups in the molecule is necessary. Some polymers 
contain groups with large dipoles, such as epoxies, amino, cyanate, isocyanides, hydroxyls, 
sulphonamides and phthalates. The materials with the best dipole mobility exhibit high absorption

efficiency and the efficiency of the coupling depends on the strength of the dipole, its mobility and mass, 
as well as its dipole matrix state. The choice of the polymers and the formulation will therefore need to be 
investigated for an optimal heating effect. By applying electromagnetic radiation to a material, movement 
of the molecules via dipole interactions with the oscillating electric field is induced, resulting in molecular 
friction. This leads to dielectric heating of materials.  
Comparison of Rf and MW heating key properties  
Parameter  Rf  MW  
Applied heating efficiency  order of 60%  order of 65%  
Uniformity of heating  Good   Can be less uniform   
Impedance matching  Can be difficult for certain applications Easily applied   
Energy delivery  Accurate and fast   Less controlled   
Remote power sources  Ok  Ok with waveguide transfer to the applicator  
Screening to comply with EMC  Easy   More difficult  requirements  
Tailor heating to materials  Easy   (More) difficult    
Common characteristics  
Volumetric heating –no external Dielectric heating does not require physical Low shear transport 
(conveyorised possible) heat transfer contact with the product  
Typical susceptors that are used nowadays for MW heating  are metaloxide nanoparticles (e.g. 
ferroelectric oxide particles  
e.g. BaTiO3, siliconcarbide, ZnO, silica or alumina). To date, use of microwave heating in carbon-based 
materials research of is limited , mainly focused on liquid solutions. However, more rapid and efficient 
heating is possible in electron -rich solid materials, because the target materials absorb energy of 
microwaves effectively and exclusively. Carbon-based solid materials are suitable for microwave-heating. 
Specifically, carbon nanotubes (CNTs) have excellent electrical properties resulting from sp 2 networks of 
carbon atoms. The number of free electrons which can migrate freely over the whole of the layer is about 
one per each carbon atom. The free electrons enable CNTs to absorb microwave energy considerably 
and to be heated effectively by microwaves 19.The tuning feasibility of the permittivity value by chang ing 
MWCNTs concentration (1-5%) has been also proven20.  
Other applications like annealing, regeneration and detection based on microwave heating of CNTs have 
been reported.  
DECOAT novelty:  
The main idea is the possibility to include susceptors in a primer layer to provide a heating effect to 
separate the substrate and the top coating. The susceptor may also be part of the actual coating in case 
of a single layer. The bulk polymer would need to be a low dielectric material to avoid MW heating of the 
bulk. The aim is to provide selective heating in the area of the susceptor, to either melt (and remove) the 
primer and thus also the coating on top, or to melt the entire coating itself and remove the coating layer.  
For the heating, we will start with MW heatin g as most likely option . Also Rf will be tested and the 
possibility to add (as extra) some radiant heating component. In DECOAT, it will be investigated which 
would be the best energy source, or combination, to use in order to achieve the effect.   
To tune the heating and to make it more uniform, we will optimise the % and type of additive. We will 
also investigate the addition of MW transparent material such as silica or alumina to disperse the particles 
and moderate the heating effect. Indeed, heating needs to be limited to prevent thermal runaway causing

melting or charring of the bulk polymer. Issues might occur if the component contains e.g. metal parts (e.g. 
fixings/brackets) which could cause arcing or materials fillers that heat strongly in a MW/Rf f ield. This 
potential variability of feed would need to be considered, even though we will try to limit the variability via 
the previous disassembly step and the monitoring and sorting of the waste.   
We will start making formulations with common susceptors  such as small metal particles (nano 
particles), ferroelectric oxide particles e.g. BaTiO 3, silicon carbide or blends with high dielectric polymer. 
The primer layer or the coating will need a lower melting point compared to the bulk material.   
A novelty for targeted MW heating will be the exploration of carbon -based 
particles, like carbon powder, MWCNT and CNT/MO (Metal Oxide) nanocomposites 
(see picture on the side21). The latter are a new generation with interesting properties 
that not only combine the p roperties of CNTs and MO but also hold new properties 
caused by the interaction between them. Additionally, use of these materials in a 
polymer coating formulation can overcome some common disadvantages. For 
example, metal oxide nanoparticles have a tendency to agglomerate because of their 
dangling bonds. In CNT/MO composites, depositing nanoparticles on the surface of 
the nanotube prevents their agglomeration. The aggregation of nanotubes in aqueous 
media is solved by functionalization. As a result, these nanocomposites are attractive for a wide range of 
applications compared to the isolated nanoparticles because CNTs act as a carrier to stabilize the 
nanoparticles, maintaining their integrity.  
This will help the induction of the heat through MW and Rf inside the coating, since CNTs will work as a 
thermal conductive network inside the polymer matrix, which should be very beneficial for the 
debonding functionality.   
(ii) Automated Sorting technology based on DECOAT triggerable additives – mainly related to WP3 
& WP4 work  
For the actual recycling of DECOAT products to be implemented in a future waste management 
configuration, an efficient and automated sorting is required based on the material composition for 
defining the respective recycling route and for s eparating them from non -recyclable parts (e.g. 
standard coated parts not including triggers). Due to the lack of recycling options, textiles are often not 
separately collected or sorted and automotive non-metallic parts shredded as a mixture that is energetically 
valorised at best, plastics (especially packaging, WEEE) are industrially sorted via spectroscopic 
identification systems (e.g. NIR, X-ray, colour, laser and combinations)35 or even hand sorting. The latter 
is unreliable and not suitable for the DECOAT materials as the smart additives cannot be seen by human 
workers. Partner IRIS has experience in developing tailored fast online VIS/NIR spectroscopic tools for 
plastic processing monitoring to identify precisely their composition and to detect traces  of unwanted 
substances22,23.   
NIR absorbance spectroscopy is well established as a technique for inline sorting of a wide range of 
materials, including agricultural products such as food products; minerals 24  and plastic waste 39. In 
particular, Steinert GmbH40 provides a wide range of sorting machines with NIR imaging, handling plastic 
waste, household waste, compost, building waste, and minerals produced by mining. Waste plastics are 
especially suited for NIR absorbance characterisation, as they exhibit strong spectral features in the near 
infra-red. Conversely, most plastics have similar appearance and shapes in the visible wavelength range, 
so using conventional machine vision for sorting is not effective. Allied with this, a large proportion of waste

Smart plastic materials with intrinsic recycling properties by design  
Research & Innovation Action addressing CE-NMBP-26-2018  
  
[DECOAT]    Page 18 of 70  
This proposal version was submitted by GUY BUYLE on 28/06/2018 16:55:43 Brussels Local Time. Issued by the Participant Portal Submission Service. 
plastic materials will be presented as macroscale objects of a single material type, which means they are 
easily imaged using the spatial resolution available with common hyperspectral cameras.   
Challenges remain in sorting plastics, including distinguishing materials that have multiple component 
materials, such as coatings, colouring agents and fillers, or separating objects that are dark coloured, or 
which have been used for containing different materials, including hazardous ones.   
Recently, the addition of fluorescent markers has been developed as a way to add specificity to 
objects that are difficult to sort based on NIR spectroscopy of their basic material type. Such organic 
dyes absorb light of a particular wavelength and emit light with a longer wavelength. This property, called 
fluorescence, can be detected with extremely high sensitivity and therefore, only a very small concentration 
of a fluorescent dye is sufficient. Coatings with a thickness of ~ 1 μm require a dye concentration in the 
order of some parts per billion, to enable the detection of the fluorescence. Such a small concentration in 
a thin layer is not visible to the eye and does not affect physical properties like mechanical strength.  
In terms of process control of transparent organic coat ings, such a system has been recently 
developed, with the involvement of partner IVV, by the Fraunhofer Polymer Surfaces Alliance POLO®25. 
For the excitation of the dye molecules a (monochromatic) light source (LED, laser) is required, and a 
digital camera can be used to detect the fluorescence light. Depending on the dye’s optical properties 
additional components such as filters might be necessary. These properties can be used to tag materials 
and coatings26 and for example determine coating thickness. In addition, some dyes show a fluorescence 
effect, whose intensity and wavelength is influenced by the surrounding molecules, the pH, the ionic  
strength of the medium, or by the viscosity of the matrix, showing a stronger fluorescence depending on 
the cross-linking degree of the resin.  
In terms of sorting, fluorescent markers are already well established as a means of sorting cells or other 
biological species27. A prototype system has been developed to sort small flakes of plastic, by the addition 
of fluorophores28, which reduces the minimum size of an object that can be sorted, and handles more 
complex polymer blends. A commercial fluorescent marker and sorting system for polymers is offered by 
Polysecure29. This system requires the addition of fluorophores to the polymer itself, and in sufficient 
quantities for detection, and so the amount of fluorescent material required is a limiting factor to the general 
use of this technology. A new product under development by Nextek Ltd., called PRISM30, is based on the 
addition of fluorescent inks to the labels of plastic containers, which strongly reduces the amount of 
fluorophore required, but presents chall enges if the labels have been partially removed or degraded in 
handling.  
Fluorescent markers are typically produced in the form of molecular dyes/ nanoparticles. Dyes are the 
most cost -effective fluorophore, which makes them attractive for waste sorting. However, where 
nanoparticles are already deployed as functional additives, such as paramagnetic oscillators, it should be 
possible to add the functionality of fluorescence to existing pa rticles, or microspheres. Such dual -use 
particles have been developed for combined cancer -cell imaging and destruction 31 . Commercial 
fluorescent paramagnetic microspheres are available32, while customised particles will be investigated that 
are suitable for material properties of debonding adhesives under development. While carbon nanotubes-
based nanocomposites are expected to require fluorescence dyes, other standard metallic nanoparticles 
(eg iron oxide) can be identified using standard X -ray fluorescence s pectroscopy without dye addition. 
Dispersion of nanoparticles in thin films can be characterised by diffractometric techniques49.

DECOAT novelty : In terms of process control, fluorescent dyes, applied in ppm (parts per million) 
concentrations in core and shell of the triggering materials, and resulting in a specific fluorescence will be 
used in the design the microcapsules. The detection system records the fluorescence intensity distribution, 
which finally will be converted into volume distribution of the core and shell within the coating. Moreover, 
the curing states of the core and shell can be imaged by using special dyes. The advantage of such a 
system is to measure and control all thickness/curing state relevant parameters at the same time during 
production. In terms of sorting, these fluorescent markers in the core -shell will be unique to the 
microcapsule and its triggering mechanism, and therefore allow sorting each part to the appropriate 
channel and process for debonding and separation. The amounts of fluorophore are limited, and localised 
to the region of the part containing the adhesive layers, so that in the case where more than one additive 
is present, they can be identified separately, and a multiple stage recycling route can be implemented. All 
in all, we will research and optimise the hardware based on tailored photonic techniques  and software 
using advanced chemometrics analysis and combining several technologies as required for 
identifying each relevant material. They will be adjusted to sort bonded and coated materials. Similar 
techniques will be used to control proper removal and quantify residues as needed to ensure quality of 
reprocessed substrate material and for the tracking important parameters of the core shell synthesis, 
nano/micro composites dispersion and coating thickness as determinants of trigger efficiency.  
(iii) Upgraded mechanical and chemical recycling process – mainly related to WP4 work  
Trigger assisted mechanical recycling . Mechanical recycling is often most preferred from  economic 
viewpoint. However, it is often limited in capabilities, e.g. for removal of contaminants. A well -known 
method is using a continuous high speed centrifuge to remove water and other impurities during plastic 
recycling. To our knowledge, combination with a trigger is not known at pilot or industrial level.  
DECOAT novelty: develop a continuous recycling pilot line to separate the coating from the coated 
plastics and textiles part  by using a high speed industrial centrifuge  (mechanical separation) th at 
integrates the trigger mechanisms and that can be combined with the novel sorting technology. The 
idea is to start with grinding in medium size parts (3 x 3 cm), to have the material pass through an activation 
step (e.g. microwave, IR or steam oven tunnel or chemical bath), to trigger the debonding and to start the 
separation process. The centrifuge movement will permit that the coating, even if its weight is comparable 
to the weight of the substrate material, will go out of the system fairly easy. Finally, the ‘decoated’ polymer 
(plastic parts, textile fibres) will be extruded and pelletized to be used again for melt extrusion. CO2 will 
be introduced during the melt extrusion process to remove residual amounts of coating and VOCs.  
Chemical recycling via  CREASOLV process : This is a solvent based selective extraction process 
developed by partner IVV that allows pure plastics at the molecular level to be recovered due to their 
specific solubility. It has no by-product (solvents can be used in closed loop en dlessly with only few ppm 
lost in each cycle) and a very low environmental impact demonstrated by peer-reviewed LCAs33,34. Unlike 
general chemical recycling that generate monomers requiring a new polymerization step, Creasolv® 
generates polymers. One further advantage is that via successive steps with tailored solvents, even mixed 
input materials can be recycled. Furthermore, due to the mild processing conditions (no supercritical 
conditions), the polymer maintains its properties as opposed to mechanical recy cling. So far, CreaSolv® 
has been applied to standard thermoplastics like PS, PE, PVC, PA, etc. It has also already been 
successfully tested on lab-scale for recycling of PVC/PET composites35.  
DECOAT novelty: the process will be adapted for typical textile and plastics coatings enabling the 
recovery of selected coating materials . In combination with DECOAT core/shell microcapsules and 
lipophilic SAPs, the CreaSolv® treatment will be researched as a potential method of triggering, e.g. by 
triggering the swel ling of SAP’s and also as a method to remove remaining contaminants after the 
application of a previous (mechanical) debonding process.

(iv) Demonstration of circular use and related eco-design– mainly related to WP5 work  
Circular use of raw materials of  coated parts. DECOAT emphasises on the removal step, i.e. the 
separation of the coating from the substrate. However, we also want to show that it is possible to recycle 
the materials, coating and substrate afterwards.   
DECOAT novelty: DECOAT ambitions to show this circular use and wants to demonstrate it for one generic 
coated textile and for one generic coated plastics case. We want to select two such generic systems and 
will show circularity of the material use. For the actual debonding, we will apply t he most suitable 
technology developed within DECOAT. For the recycling of the coating and of the substrate material, we 
will rely on the best available technologies at that moment.   
2000  
Difficult to predict already now but some good candidates: PVC coated PES fabric and ABS coated PUR. 
The actual recycling of the thermoset PUR is outside the scope of DECOAT but several efforts (eg H2020 
projects URBANREC, see also section 2.1.2) are ongoing. DECOAT will rely on these efforts for showing 
the circular use.  
Further, we want to also make prototypes of actual parts for the use cases presented by the end -users, 
see also next section. These prototypes will be based on eco -design of the coatings,  implementing the 
DECOAT findings. The outcome will be strategies to design single and multilayer coating solutions for 
textile and plastic substrates using our DECOAT developments.  
A public tool will be released that helps to integrate DECOAT technology in the eco-design of coated parts.  
  
1.4.2 Innovation potential  
The innovation potential of DECOAT is rooted in the current need for improved high tech material 
solutions for implementation of cost efficient solutions for recycling of coated textile and plastic 
parts. A significant contribution can be delivered to the emerging remanufacturing industry in 
Europe by helping it to extend to plastic parts.  
Recycling of coated plastic parts. The current technology for recycling painted parts is 
based on chemical bath, which is expensive, prone to contamination, and difficult to 
implement on small scale (so for SMEs that need to recycle the scrap). The technology 
is only available in some large recycling companies and OEMs. This chemical bath has a 
removal efficiency of ca 95-98,5%. The current 1,5% of coating that remains causes a 
dramatic loss of properties in the recycled material . Taking all this into account, the 
innovation potential targeted by DECOAT is to (i) find alternatives for the current chemical baths 
for a more environmental -friendly process that is easier and cheaper to implement at small 
scale/companies; (ii) achieve a removal efficiency of >99% in a single step, leading to higher quality 
recycled material at market affordable price.   

Smart plastic materials with intrinsic recycling properties by design  
Research & Innovation Action addressing CE-NMBP-26-2018  
  
[DECOAT]    Page 21 of 70  
This proposal version was submitted by GUY BUYLE on 28/06/2018 16:55:43 Brussels Local Time. Issued by the Participant Portal Submission Service. 
Market size coated plastics: European plastic parts production was almost € 37billion euro (Eurostat – 
2016 data). Assuming that ca 20% is coated, we find a European market value for coated plastic parts 
of more than €7 billion . For the coating materials themselves (acrylates, 
polyurethanes,…), the worldwide market on plastics w as valued at ca. € 5 
billion (2015). The global demand for automotive coatings is ca. 3 million 
tonnes, with the European share over 20% (2016)36.   
Recycling of coated textiles . In the past recycling of PVC coated PET 
fabrics and pilot scale recycling has been tried, using chemical recycling, 
e.g. TexyLoop54. Further, textile recycling mainly focusses on shredding of 
uncoated textile fabrics (typically production waste, also some post -
consumer) into short fibres that are reused for non -woven production. And 
although coated textiles are often used, no economically viable solutions 
targeting a reasonable value after recycling exist today. The innovation 
potential targeted by  
DECOAT is to present coating solutions made with recycling in mind, both 
from the design and materials side. Moreover, the corresponding techniques 
will be developed. This innovation potential is on medium-term as DECOAT 
will offer a novel option, in-between the existing pure mechanical and 
chemical recycling, for dealing with coated textiles and plastics.  
Market size coated textiles : The global coated fabrics market is 
projected to reach almost €25 billion by 2021 , at a CAGR of more than 
4% in terms of value, during the forecast period, 2016 to 2021 37. The 
European textile coatings market is projected to witness growth over 
4% up to 2024. Rising industrial, transportation, and building & construction 
are driving the market for textile coatings. Along with these, increasing 
quality and performance standards in textile coating based fabrics a re also 
driving the market. Recent market potential of microencapsulated 
products/ additives is estimated at ca. €5 billion, growth ca 5 -7%/y38 and ca. €1 billion within the 
technical textile and clothing industry39, focusing on applications like medical, sportswear and leisure.  
The global textile coating binders market is dominated by thermoplastics (2015 data): PVC-Polyvinyl 
Chloride (ca 40%), Polyurethane (ca. 20%) and Acrylics (ca. 7%). PVC is the most used coating for various 
applications due to its low cost, easy weldability, and easy coloration and printability. PU benefits from 
being an eco -friendly textile coa ting, and is widely accepted and preferred by manufacturers in North 
America and Europe. Applications for coated textiles are very diverse, but main areas are: 
transportation, building and construction  and (technical) clothing , e.g. for outdoor, workwear, 
protective clothing40.   
Although currently limited, there is a strong driver for using coated textiles for daily clothing as it has 
been shown that the coating is reducing microfibre losses during washing by up to 50% 41. But, 
having to deal with coatings would hamper and delay the current emerging recycling of textiles even more. 
This is a potentially very large opportunity DECOAT technology can tap into.  
Recycling of automotive plastics. During the process of recycling ELV (End -of-Life Vehicles), most of 
the metal is recovered from cars, and the rest of materials and parts are shredded to obtain what is called

ASR (automotive shredder residue) 42. This ASR represents 20 -25% of the original car weight, it 
contains typically 20 to almost 50% plastics and most of it is currently landfilled or burned. The EU 
directive (2000/53/EC), demands that only 5% of total ELV is landfilled. The plastic separation in the ASR 
is complex and the purity that is currently feasibly is not enough to have productive mechanical properties 
in the recycled plastic. It is stated that “ The recovery [of plastic material] has remained marginal; yet 
it should develop rapidly now, given the EU requirements regarding material recycling. Manual dismantling 
allows cleaner recovery, yet it is severely limited by productivity …. Conversely, it seems illusory to recover 
really clean materials, such as PUR, after passing them through a shredder, even though washing 
procedures have been tested ”60. Therefore, rather than focussing on small incremental 
improvements, DECOAT proposes a radical novel approach.  
The global automotive industry is set to reach 114 million cars in worldwide sales annually by 2024, 
with an expected growth on average of 2.8%43. Within the EC report ’GEAR 2030‘44 (2017), the opportunity 
for building novel or extending existing value chains for a circular automotive sector is highlighted, as well 
the possibility for novel business models, eg for the recycling or re -use of (spare) parts. DECOAT feeds 
directly into this opportunity, by providing novel technological solutions for both disassembly and recycling. 
The high-end premium car segment which uses a lot of aesthetics coatings  (ca. 13% of MAIER’s 
turnover) is ‘booming’: it increased with more than 50% from 2010 to 201645.   
Of special interest for the automotive sector is the increased use of composite parts, especially in 
electric vehicles. This currently hampers the EoL processing as recycling of composite parts is much 
more difficult as (coated) metal parts. Therefore vehicles are designed that are easier to recycle, eg by 
making them easier to (partially) disassemble, even in a robotised manner. This offers a huge opportunity 
for the recycling industry and linked activities. DECOAT technology for recycling parts fits perfectly in this 
scheme.  
Opportunities exist also for trucks and busses , the latter are covered by partner MBT. Although less 
common then cars, there are roughly 1 million so -called HDOR (Heavy duty and off -road vehicles) 
discarded every year. Disassembly is much more common and far advanced with some brands for metal 
parts (iron, steel, aluminium, up to ca 75-85% of the total weight). Remaining challenge is dealing with 
complex multi-material parts (with electronics). DECOAT technology could contribute.   
Emerging market: recycling of smart textiles and plastics with integrated electronics . The market 
for smart textiles and wearables is expected to enter an exponential growth phase from an expected 
total world market of nearly 1.5 bn US$ by 2021 46 to almost 3 bn US$ for the EU market alone in 202447. 
Eg DECOAT partners MBT, MAIER and CTAG are working on integrating electronics into various 
(interior) car elements. Next to the actual manufacturing, a missing solution to recycle the smart plastics 
hampers the market introduction. Therefore, we will also look into this emerging automotive application.   
Remanufacturing is the process of returning a used product to its original performance specifications 
(from the customers’ view). It is a strategy to reduce manufacturing's environmental impacts while boosting 
profitability. If considered during the early stages of a product's design, the concept of ‘active disassembly’ 
can be applied enabling the rapid and non -destructive disassembly of products with minimal component 
damage, which facilitates remanufacturing. DECOAT technology can aid to implement active disassembly, 
easing the actual disassembly (DECOAT – 1st level) and for making it easier to upgrade used parts by 
removing the coating so that it can be recoated. Remanufacturing is especially of interest for the 
automotive market. The worldwide automotive parts remanufacturing market (focus on metal parts) was 
valued at US$ 33.16 Billion in  
2016 and is estimated to grow at a CAGR of 6.6% from 2017 to 202548. DECOAT technology could help 
to extend it also to plastics parts. Note that this is much stronger than the actual a utomotive market

(worldwide 2.8%, in EU only 1.7%). Economic benefits for both remanufacturing companies as well as the 
customers are considered as the biggest driver for the overall automotive parts remanufacturing market. 
Remanufacturing aids in reducing  cost pertaining to major parameters such as material, machining, 
production time, energy, fuel and labour costs, among other. In addition, present -day automobiles are 
made flexible so that they can easily incorporate remanufactured parts without affecting the performance 
of the vehicle.  
Remanufacturing goes wider than the automotive sector: the EU Remanufacturing Network estimates 
the current size of the EU remanufacturing industry to be almost €30bn, employing around 190,000 
people. Their market study shows that the industry could triple to €90bn by 2030 and employ as many 
as 255,000 people 49. As DECOAT technology will also be able to apply on large full part s, it can 
contribute to and benefit from the remanufacturing opportunity . The ERN study 68 identified the top 
barriers for remanufacturing becoming more widespread, i.e. volume/availability of a ‘core’ (used part 
intended to become a remanufactured product)  and quality of the core. For coated parts, DECOAT 
technology answers to those barriers. The aerospace sector was shown as the sector where 
remanufacturing is currently being used most. On 2 nd and 3rd place are automotive and EEE (Electrical 
and Electronic Equipment). The INDAR technology of partner RESCOLL is indeed already emerging in 
the aerospace sector. Our goal is to make it also enter other sectors, like automotive and EEE. This shows 
that DECOAT will also be useful for further development for disass embly of parts for these sectors (and 
debonding of coatings).  
The global adhesives market was valued at ca. €35billion in 2015 and is projected to reach ca. €50billion 
by 202150. The share of debondable adhesives is nowadays only ca. 0.5% of the general adhesives 
market. The water -borne adhesives (= DECOAT scope) account for ca 50% of the global market. If 
DECOAT results reach 1% of this market, we can address a global market of ca. €170-250million.  
The above motivates the selection of the DECOAT use cases: watertight backpack for outdoor gear 
(VAUDE), power switches and sockets for household electronics (PANASONICS) and automotive 
parts (with integrated electronics) (MBT, MAIER) as applications and sectors where recycling of 
coated materials is needed but no economically viable solutions are available yet.

not only to achieve knowledge advancements, but also to proceed in technological advancements in terms 
of patenting. The survey was conducted in the spirit of investigating and detecting unexplored areas and 
approaches of the field and to avoid possible overlapping with existed protected intellectual knowledge. 
The consortium decided that although a number of patents exist in similar fields, DECOAT project offers 
a huge opportunity to achieve technological advancements beyond the state of the art.

2 Impact  
‘Do not go where the path may lead, go instead where there is no path and 
leave a trail’  (Ralph Waldo Emerson, writer)
2.1 Expected impacts   
DECOAT fits extremely well in the European Circular Economy strategy as it aims for a systemic 
innovation for the recycling of coated textile and plastic materials. The materials solutions will 
build further on latest (carbon) nanotechnology, microencapsul ation and polymer developments 
and lead to several scientific and technological breakthroughs. DECOAT results will create new 
markets for triggerable additives, optical material detection systems and for recycling equipment 
and processes. Together this amo unts to over 150 million annual turnover, i.e. the potential to 
directly create ca. 500 jobs.  Moreover, DECOAT materials and process innovations will give a 
competitive edge to a relevant share of the European companies active along the coated textiles 
and plastics value chains that could generate a total turnover of ca. €1.7 billion, implying  that 
DECOAT would further help to secure ca 5.000 jobs.  
2.1.1 Expected impacts set out in the work programme  
In the subsections below, we list how DECOAT will resp ond to each of the expected impacts of the call. 
We focus on the impact within and beyond DECOAT and try to illustrate both aspects.  
2.1.1.1 Meeting the EU's circular economy and environmental targets while demonstrating a clear benefit, 
i.e. more efficient or economic than the state of the art in order to enable market uptake in the 
short to medium term  
Smart plastic materials with intrinsic recycling properties by design  
Research & Innovation Action addressing CE-NMBP-26-2018  
  
[DECOAT]    Page 26 of 70  
This proposal version was submitted by GUY BUYLE on 28/06/2018 16:55:43 Brussels Local Time. Issued by the Participant Portal Submission Service. 
Efficiency aspect: The current disposal methods for coated textiles and plastics are landfilling, 
incineration or (very limitedly) chemical bath recyclin g, that all have severe disadvantages.  
Incineration51 conventionally leads to the release of significant greenhouse gas CO2, pollutants like NOx, 
SO2, and particulate matter. Additionally, heavy metals and dioxins are emitted. Given the high energy 
content, much of the heat is recovered as energy source. However, the residual product consists of bottom 
ash, fly ash, and air pollution control residue, which is currently also landfilled. The incineration process 
involves a flue gas cleaning process, which may give rise to externalities to wastewater. For landfill70 
disposal, the main emissions are CO2 and CH4. Moreover, leachate is released to the surrounding soil and 
water. In addition to environmental impact, both incineration and landfill processes require f acilities with 
serious disadvantages, e.g. for the people living in the vicinity. Chemical baths are currently used to 
remove coatings and to recycle the left -over fraction but they are very limited and cumbersome from 
environmental side (see section 1.4.2 ). These dramatic impacts will be avoided by the DECOAT 
innovations, as instead of being disposed of, the majority of the original material can get a secondary life-
cycle. This saves on the production of virgin material, thus contributing to significantly reducing emissions 
throughout the value chain.   
DECOAT offers a solution to coated plastics and textiles recycling via the development of triggered 
debonding of coatings via improved mechanical or chemical recycling, resulting in  a highly promising, 
efficient alternative to current recycling and material design standards.  
Economic aspect:  for doing the extra sorting and activation of the trigger, an additional cost during 
recycling of ca €80 -100/ton is estimated (including OPEX and CAPEX), largely depending on the 
stimulation mechanism (e.g. infrared vs microwave). This is an economic attra ctive proposition as the 
current process of non-coated plastics recycling, including extrusion and pretreatment (grinding, washing, 
drying) costs ca. €300 -400/ton. So, coated parts recycling can be enabled by ca. 20% extra cost 
compared to non-coated parts, which is close to but below the only current solution (solvolysis ~ 
€500/ton).  Contribution to EU’s circular economy and environmental targets , which specifically 
recommends accounting for the entire plastics value chain 6. The primary focus of the EU ci rcular 
economy for plastic use and production is to promote the reuse, repair, and recycling needs of plastic 
value chains through enhanced sustainable design. DECOAT directly addresses these challenges by 
developing a smarter initial design to plastic and  textile coating that can be removed upon end -of-life 
status, allowing a high percentage of the original material to go on to a second life -cycle. This directly 
supports the EU’s goals to achieve a “modern, low-carbon, resource and energy-efficient economy” while 
making “a tangible contribution to reaching the 2030 Sustainable Development Goals and the Paris 
Agreement”.  
2.1.1.2 Create new technologies and business opportunities for the recycling industry across Europe, 
especially in the area of composites and plastics where the challenge is high  
Key DECOAT result: novel technologies for triggerable coatings (

share of debondable adhesives is nowadays only ca. 0.5% or ca. €65 million of the general 
adhesives market. Our results will contribute to increase this market share as we expect to generate a 
market of DECOAT triggerable additives of ca. €20 million (see part D2 of the PEDR). The DECOAT 
partners will be ideally positioned to capitalise on this growing market: RESCOLL expects the project to 
lay the basis for a new product line of debondable adhesives and primers for textiles and plastics products. 
RESCOLL has the capacity to scale-up to production via its subsidiary company STILZ Chimie to which it 
wants to transfer the developed technology (royalty based agreement), and which is currently capable of

garments. The use of more sustainable and recyclable materials opens up commercial opportunities as

equipment (optical detection, MW equipment, Creasolv system), which is estimated in total at €35million.

data); textile & clothing sector: 1.7 million jobs (EURATEX data). It is calculated in part D2 of the PEDR

companies active all along the coated textiles and plastics value chains that is relevant to a total 
turnover of ca. €1.7 billion, implying that DECOAT will help to secure ca 5.000 jobs.   
2.1.1.3 Demonstrate a potential reduction in landfill waste volume by > 50%  
DECOAT will lead to a decrease in landfilling by an estimated 75% as the largest fraction of coated 
parts (the bulk) will be re -used. Indeed, the coating itself does (in most applications ) not relevantly 
participate to the volume. Nevertheless, to account for potential damage at the coating interface, we 
assume a 5% loss of the bulk volume. Depending on the type of recycling used in combination with the 
trigger, it might be possible to recycle the coating material. However, here we assume the coating itself is 
not recuperated, so 5% volume remains landfilled. One further element to take into account is that the 
recycling process of the bulk itself will not be perfect. A fair estimate is to assume 80% efficiency. So, ca. 
75% of the volume of the initial coated waste can be recycled, see also the concrete case study in section 
2.1.4.  
This reduction is relevant for in total almost 3.5 million tons of coated plastic from cars, electrical 
and electronic equipment and coated textiles. This number is calculated based on the following 
data and estimates53:  
• Automotive parts: ca. 2.1 million tons of coated plastic come from end -of-life vehicles (ELV) 
per year. Motivation: 27 million cars are discarded every year 73, at 225 kg plastic per car (15% of 
average total car weight74) leads to ca. 6 million tons plastics ELV from cars of which ca. 35% is coated/

of the DECOAT consortium 73 Automotive Recycling. Devalued is now revalued’ Plastic Market watch. SPI (2016) 74 ‘Automotive 
The world moves with plastics". Plastic Europe (2013)

• Heavy vehicles parts: ca. 800 ktons of coated plastics. Motivation: ca. 1 million heavy duty vehicles

acrylic and PVC (=DECOAT scope) is ca 70%. Furthermore, due to improved collection practices, we 
estimate that ca 50% of the products will be available for recycling (currently only 25%58 but increasing 
trend), which leads to ca. 350 ktons.   
2.1.1.4  Reduction of the carbon footprint of the corresponding products by > 30% (based on a full Life 
Cycle Assessment).  
The actual carbon footprint, and other relevant environmental parameters, will be calculated during 
DECOAT via a Life Cycle Assessment (LCA). The study will include all life cycle stages as represented in 
the figure below, impacts of each of them shall be considered during the assessment. At this moment, we 
estimate the potential of the proposed technologies for carbon footprint reduction. To do so, we consider 
the first use of virgin materials in an open loop product system, wher e no changes occur in the 
inherent properties of the recycled material. Here, the “business as usual” baseline scenario 
comprises the assumed current End -of-Life (EoL) treatment (landfill and incineration), and the 
DECOAT scenario comprises recycling of th e bulk material from the products in their EoL . This 
recycling would allow to receive the “emission credits” due to avoided emissions during the virgin bulk 
material production that the recycled material is intended to replace (avoided burden approach). Th ese 
credits are deducted from the total impact of the product as a part of the EoL stage. In exchange, the 
impacts of the triggerable debonding process and the average composition of the coated parts to be 
recycled are also allocated to the product system.   
  
 
  
The emissions from logistics, use, andfill disposal and incineration are assumed to be equal for both EoL 
scenarios, and thus not included in the inventory for the current calculations. This and all other 
methodological choices will be further analysed and evaluated in dedicated sensitivity analyses during the 
execution of the full LCA. Thus, the total impact can be expressed as follows:

On the generalized level, the relative reduction of 
environmental impacts due to DECOAT introduction can be 
expressed as a function of the bulk material recovery 
efficiency (recycling rate), and a contribution of bulk material 
to the total product’s enviro nmental impact. The distribution 
of relative reduction values dependent on the two parameters 
is presented in the graph on the right.  
With the assumptions made about the contribution of the bulk 
material into the total environmental impact of the product 
(80%) it is necessary then to keep the recycling rate 
(efficiency of recycling) above 62.5%. However, for the target 
applications, the B/T ratio if not expected to in the lower 
ranges, and the recycling rate is expected to be designed 
above 95%. Thus, the products’ performance will realistically 
always be in area on right upper side of dashed line, i.e. we will comply with and even outperform this 
expected impact.  Additional benefits that were not included would further reduce the environmental 
impact during waste handling i.e. the reduced emissions that result from not incinerating or landfilling the 
materials, and possible recycling of the coating material.  
Under current circumstances, the production of 1kg of coated PVC results in 2.73 kgCO2/kg virgin material. 
The DECOAT process would cut the carbon emission by 46%  (1.46 kgCO2/kg virgin material), 
assuming that the DECOAT process has a material recovery efficien cy EffB of 80% (this reflects a 20% 
loss due to processing energy and lost base material during processing). This also means the DECOAT 
process would result in a 76% reduction in waste generation (80% efficiency results in 0.76 kg virgin 
material recovered per kg virgin material input into the system). These initial outcomes of the case study 
show that DECOAT expected impacts exceed the desired 30% reduction in carbon footprint and 
also the 50% reduction of landfilling (see section 2.1.3).   
  
2.1.2 Improving innovation capacity and market creating potential  
Innovation capacity comprises the internal ability to respond to external opportunities with an 
innovative solution that leads to profitable business. DECOAT claims to significantly increase the 
innovation capacity in the coatings, adhesives and paintings sector and the related value chain 
(material suppliers, end users) by  combining two external opportunities: (i) the global need for 
recyclable coated parts (= market pull) and (ii) the large(r) scale a nd low(er) cost availability of 
smart multifunctional additives such as nano- and microparticles (carbon-based among others) (= 
technology push). DECOAT developments and results aim at capitalising these challenges, both from 
pure technical standpoint as from supporting activities (standardisation, LCA, benchmarking) as described 
below.   
DECOAT will  boost the innovation capacity of the debonding technology providing partners 
(RESCOLL, DEVAN, CTECH, IRIS, MAIER) by demonstrating the advantages of their materials and 
processes for different use cases for VAUDE, PANASONIC, MBT and MAIER. A direct advantage for 
the end -users will be the reduced cost for sourcing material as the cost of recycled material (at ca. 
€500/ton, see section 2.1.1.1) will be lower as virgin ones.

medium to long term.

For realising the DECOAT impact, a 
crucial part is that waste streams 
with triggerable coatings have to be 
made available in suitable form and 
in sufficient amounts . There are two 
main inputs: production waste and EoL 
post-consumer waste. We have 3 
streams to consider: coated textiles, 
consumer electronics components and 
automotive (see table with overview).   
For production waste, there should 
be no issues: triggerable coated items should be collected separately , which is no problem as 
manufacturing companies have now already several separate waste types they collect. Logically, it will 
take some years before sufficient products with DECOAT-based technology reaches the EoL stage. 
The  
real strength of the DECOAT technologies is exactly that it is also suite d for EoL because the triggerable 
coated waste can be detected and thus processed separately.   
We illustrate the case of coated outdoor textile products . On short term, partner VAUDE will offer 
its clients the option to send back its EoL products that include DECOAT technology. That way, items 
will be grouped together and can be recycled. On medium term, collectors of EoL textile (like group De 
Boer Recycling Group – ASG member) will have to install the optical detection equipment for triggerable 
coatings and thus separate this waste stream. Then, they can send it to recyclers (transitional phase) or 
recycle it themselves (longer term). This will require them to extend their current recycling equipment with 
equipment based on the DECOAT recycling plant.   
Also for the recycling of coated electronics parts, an extension of the current waste processing 
equipment to separate triggerable coatings and to channel that fraction to DECOAT recycling lines. 
This will be implemented by players like REMONDIS (member of DECOAT ASG). Full recyclability of its 
products will be a competitive advantage for PANASONIC.   
For the automotive parts, the situation is more complex and DECOAT has to rely on supporting 
developments in vehicle disassembly . Indeed, currently only a smal l fraction of the vehicles is 
dismantled, only hazardous components or very valuable ones. The rest is all together shredded and 
separated. Focus in that process is on the recycling of metals, the rest is called the ASR or automotive 
shredder residue (see also 1.4.2) and is a highly mixed stream, containing also (some) shredded coated 
scrap. To avoid low yield and need to separate shredded fractions of very mixed composition, our recycling 
technology should be fed with disassembled coated vehicle parts. Although the latter is not evident at the 
moment, there are good foresights that this will be the case in the future. DECOAT will help partner 
MAIER to be prepared for this novel market situation. Furthermore, DECOAT will seek synergies with 
the project Multi Cycle, just approved in the SPIRE10 call, coordinated by IRIS and counting several 
partners in common, where similar challenges will be faced in terms of identifying and dismantling specific 
(in that case composite) parts prior to recycling. For support in  this matter, we have included car 
dismantling company GBP in the ASG.  
Furthermore, an ongoing development is the robotic disassembly of cars driven by the need for a 
more thorough car disassembly, especially with the growing share of electrical vehicles 60. In fact, the 
fraction of metal is decreasing in cars, so there is a need to recycle more of the other fractions to keep 
with the increasingly stringent ELV directives. The latter is often only feasible if the (valuable) parts are 
dismantled beforehand. Manual dismantling is not evident, therefore efforts are ongoing for robotisation, 
focus on removing electronics parts. Such logical development in sustainable manufacturing and
