A special case is formed by HDOR (Heavy Duty and Off Road vehicles), which includes busses and 
trucks. There, disassembly and remanufacture is already more installed than for standard (light) vehicles. 
For example, more than 93% o f all the materials in a standard DAF truck can now be reused and the 
plastic parts of a DAF truck can easily be separated during dismantling75. DECOAT results will help 
partner MBT who is specialized in bus manufacturing, and via the mother company DAIMLER also 
in trucks, in working further in this direction.  
Europe has a leading position in high quality, engineered items like coated textiles and plastics . 
Next to the actual DECOAT partners, we want to make the wider European textiles, plastics and coatin g 
and adhesives industry aware of the novel possibilities offered by the triggerable coatings and debonding 
and helping them to make the transition. For easing this transition, DECOAT will develop the eco -design 
tool, which means that the service providing SME partners IRES and ECOMAT will get a chance to boost 
their activities. Consultancy will also be given by the DECOAT RTD partners (CTB - textiles, AIMPLAS –
plastics & recycling sector, IVV, CTAG- automotive and NTUA – microcaps, nanomaterials). For example, 
AIMPLAS will be well-placed to offer engineering and consultancy to recycling companies wanting to adopt 
DECOAT technologies, via upgrading their current recycling facilities, or via building new ones. We will 
prepare the EU industry to switch to the next generation of coatings that can debond on demand and help 
them to implement this all along the value chain. We will give the European textiles, plastics, coatings 
and recycling sectors an important and lasting competitive advantage via triggered debon ding 
solutions.   
We will specifically engage in close collaboration with other related projects (such as MULTICYCLE, 
see also above and Task 7.5) and look into common publications/events to  raise awareness towards 
relevant target groups on the emerging recycling possibilities for coated textiles and plastics.   
2.1.3 Environmental and social impact – policy framework  
Environmental impact of DECOAT is tapping into the sustainable development policies of the EU, which 
is committed to a ‘high level of protection and improvement of the quality of the environment’ (Article 3 of 
the Treaty on European Union). As it has been shown in section 2.1.1.4, the suggestions of the DECOAT 
project can facilitate reduction of greenhouse gases emissions and amount of the generated waste to be 
landfilled or incinerated. The potential for waste generation reduction is ca.  
2.1 million tons for automotive parts, ca 200 kilotons for electronic goods, and ca 175 kilotons for 
coated textiles. That refers to including these amounts of the recycled material to the material recovery 
level of the waste management pyramid compared to the lower levels of energy recovery and landfillin g. 
This is especially relevant in view of the current EU waste policies and regulations (see 2.1.4, including an 
overall trend for developing circularity and for landfilling ban in some European countries (such as 
presumed by Austrian and German Landfilling Ordinances). Moreover, the estimated amounts of material 
recovery have a very large GHG emissions reduction potential. Under Article 191 of the Treaty on the 
Functioning of the European Union, combating climate change is an explicit objective of EU environmental 
policy. Under this goal, EU is pursuing the targets of reducing GHG emissions by 40% by 2030 and by 
80% by 205061. This significant contribution to reducing global warming potential of manufacturing activities 
in Europe could be also complimented by the reduction of the GHG emissions coming from the incineration 
or landfilling of the coated parts and materials. Such for example, the avoided emissions from coated 
plastics recycling (assuming PVC reduction potential for all coated plastics) could be e stimated equal to 
175*1.27=222tons CO2eq.  
A recent study conducted by Material Economics62, shows how circular business models, re-circulation of 
materials and greater material efficiency of products are the keys to build a competitive industrial economy 
with zero net emissions in Europe. All these keys are being addressed by DECOAT. To achieve this 
objective, three types of circular strategies  associated to each other have been envisaged to reduce 
                                                
61 https://ec.europa.eu/clima/policies/strategies/2050_en  
62 https://www.ellenmacarthurfoundation.org/circular-economy

EU industrial emissions: (i) the reuse of different materia ls, (ii) the reduction of the amount of materials 
that are discarded in the manufacturing or construction process, (iii) the creation of new business models 
that reinvent the way we provide services and goods. In practice, these broad strategies translate into a 
wide range of measures: changes in product design and materials choice, improved technology, 
modernized value chains, etc. The benefits of circular approaches are strengthened by the fact that 
many of these measures are complementary and that many o f these abatement opportunities are 
economically attractive. A more circular economy would have many other benefits as well, such as 
reduced geopolitical risks, local job creation, lower air pollution, and reduced water use.  
Social impact  of DECOAT is bas ed on the reduction of waste generation and of the system carbon 
emissions into human health and general well -being improvements. Basic regulations regarding H&S 
issues include arrangements for implementing the health and safety measures, identified by the  risk 
assessment, set up emergency procedures, provide clear information and training to employees. 
Moreover, recycling initiatives and businesses based on technical solutions and business models 
suggested by DECOAT will increase GDPs and create work place s (respective to the EU policies in 
employment and labour market) and stimulating local economies (Small business Act). The suggested 
DECOAT recycling schemes have a high potential to tap into the EU Social Economy framework (Social 
Business Initiative, Social Innovation policy making, Single Markets Acts 1 and 2). Additionally, the GHG 
emissions reduction due to the implementation of DECOAT solutions has a potential to avoid otherwise 
necessary measures for climate change adaptation, including in local com munities and groups with high 
vulnerability.  
DECOAT results will help to achieve several European policies,  in waste reduction, climate change 
action and mitigation, and social impact (see also section 2.1.1). DECOAT also fits in the Circular 
Economy Package consisting of an EU Action Plan for the Circular Economy that establishes a concrete 
and ambitious programme of action, with measures covering the whole cycle: from production and 
consumption to waste management and the market for secondary raw materia ls. The DECOAT 
developments on automated sorting fit the EU strategy for digitisation the manufacturing industry.  
2.1.4 External conditions influencing the expected impact  
Current low market demand for recycled plastics , which is limited to 6% of plasti cs demand in 
Europe is discouraging private investments in recycling capacities . In the EU, uptake of recycled 
plastics in new products is low and often remains limited to low -value or niche applications. One of the 
reasons for the low use of recycled plastics is the misgivings of many product brands and manufacturers, 
who fear that recycled plastics will not meet their needs for a reliable, high-volume supply of materials with 
constant quality specifications. The European Commission will promote the develo pment of 
international standards to boost industry confidence in the quality of recyclable or recycled 
plastics. It will also explore more targeted sectoral interventions in key sectors with high potential 
like packaging, construction and automotive.   
Legislation and regulations on recycling, re -use and waste form important drivers, potentially 
further enhanced by public procurement. There are several European directives on these issues that 
directly affect the production methods and materials choice of electronics consumer goods and cars. For 
the DECOAT sectors, following directives are crucial:   
• Waste Framework Directive (Directive 2008/98/EC) aims for a European recycling society with a high 
level of resource efficiency by increasing re-use and recycling of waste from households to a minimum 
of overall 50 % by weight by 2020.  
• Waste Directive – Electrical and Electronic Equipment (Directive 2012/19/EU). Waste of electrical and 
electronic equipment (WEEE); eg computers, TV-sets and fridges is a fast growi ng waste streams in 
the EU, expected to grow to more than 12 million tonnes by 2020. Overall aim is to recycle at least 
85% of electrical and electronics waste equipment by 2016 in EU.  
• European Directive 2005/64/EC on the type-approval of motor vehicles with regard to their reusability, 
recyclability and recoverability) state that 85 % of the mass of the vehicles included in the M1 and N1 
categories (passenger cars and pickup trucks) must be recyclable/reusable.  
Smart plastic materials with intrinsic recycling properties by design  
Research & Innovation Action addressing CE-NMBP-26-2018  
  
[DECOAT]    Page 37 of 70  
This proposal version was submitted by GUY BUYLE on 28/06/2018 16:55:43 Brussels Local Time. Issued by the Participant Portal Submission Service. 
• With respect to heavy duty vehicles, including busses and trucks, there are currently no End-of-life 
regulations that impose mandatory recycling and/or recovery targets75.  
These directives (or the lack of) pose challenges (and opportunities) for the autom otive and consumer 
goods sector. The DECOAT developments will help PANASONIC, MBT and MAIER to use renewable 
materials in (parts of) their products, thus working towards the directives.  
The Circular Economy Action Plan 63 aims at improving the uptake of secondary raw materials . 
DECOAT exactly aims for this by overcoming existing barriers for coated plastics material . 
DECOAT goal is to enable recycling and thus re-use of raw materials used for making coated parts. Thus, 
DECOAT is in  line with the 7th Environment Action Programme 85 and with the recently published  
‘Roadmap to Resource efficient Europe64’ which aims at promoting waste recycling and re-use. In the 
context of ongoing and upcoming evaluations of EU rules on construction pro ducts and on end -of-life 
vehicles, the EC will look into specific ways of promoting this. In the context of future work on the Packaging 
and Packaging Waste Directive, thought will also be given to using economic instruments to reward the 
use of recycled content in the packaging sector. Finally, the Commission will work on integrating recycled 
content in Green Public Procurement criteria (European Commission, 2018).  
Generally, these directives and other (national, regional) legislation can have a strong po sitive influence 
on market opportunities for use of recycling content. As an example that will be applied to other sectors: 
As a result of the implementation of the Plastic Bags Directive (Directive (EU) 2015/720) some EU Member 
States have already started to set requirements for the uptake of recycled content in plastic carrier bags. 
For instance, the Spanish Royal Decree 293/2018 establishes that from 1 January 2020 plastic carrier 
bags with a foil thickness larger than or equal to 50 µm shall be made from at least 50 % recycled plastic.  
This shows that DECOAT will place the participating companies in pole position for when the 
(legislation enforced) need for more use of recycled material becomes even higher.  
Evolving recycling landscape  
However large the need and however impressive the targets, today only 6% of new plastic materials 
used stems from recycling 65. Also within textile industry the use of recycled material is limited: for 
clothing only about 1% is closed loop, ca 12% cascade recycling 3. A gre at deal of work is underway to 
improve this (also via European projects), focussing on technical, economic and logistics aspects. As a 
result, we assume in the near future well-established networks throughout Europe where waste is 
collected and brought to local recycling facilities.  
Current recycling schemes for plastics from WEEE and ELV treatment rely mainly on post shredder 
treatment approaches since only large scale products are subjected to manual dismantling due to 
economic reasons. Coated products of concern in the DECOAT project might be too small (e.g. switches) 
or too inaccessible (e.g. dashboards) for manual dismantling. Thus, identification of coated plastics in 
plastic shred by automated detection systems will be key for future access to coated  plastics. DECOAT 
will therefore investigate optical identification tools for coated items to be implemented in 
sophisticated WEEE or ELV sorting technologies (WP3). This technology will ensure the future 
access to EoL products containing DECOAT ready-to-recycling coating systems.   
Textiles and especially complex textiles as backpacks are not recovered in specific waste streams and 
currently end up in landfills (if allowed) or incineration plants. However, the high durability of such products 
qualifies them for an EoL treatment by re-use or repair companies. Such entities are intended to be future 
sources of complex EoL textile waste such as the ones addressed in the DECOAT framework and which 
will be investigated in detail.   
Reluctance of adopting recycled materials in the industry because of specific processing needs, 
lower performance and/or early negative experiences. Several companies did already first trials with 
recycled materials and experienced difficulties in their processing, e.g. on reproducible quality levels or in 
processing. These first negative experiences, even if not valid anymore with the current materials, often 
remain and keep people biased towards further trials with recycled materials. The DECOAT technology

Cost of novel solutions and cost for recycling process/recycled materials  
DECOAT solutions will mean that the coatings will be more expensive at initial production phase (ca 10 -
15% as rough estimate). This is often rather small fraction of the total cost of the product, eg the bike bags. 
Further, gain will come because of less waste, ability to reuse materials. Therefore, rely on the emerging 
new business models and schemes like EPR – Extended Producer Responsibility, increase cost for waste 
disposal, etc.  
General economic situation . The au tomotive and household appliances sectors are cyclic sectors, 
implying that they strongly depend on the general economic situation. This can influence the sales 
foresights, especially for MAIER, MBT and PANASONIC. Currently, economic conditions in the US a nd 
Europe are high but in the future a higher market fragmentation for the industry due to the emerging 
economies is coming into play, making innovations even more important. Companies driven by innovation 
in the area of circular/recycling will beyond doubt benefit and maintain market share. Therefore the coated 
parts recycling solutions offered by DECOAT will help to become more resilient against economic 
fluctuations.
2.2 Measures to maximise impact  
(a) Dissemination and exploitation of results  
To ensure successful exploitation of the DECOAT results, dissemination and exploitation activities will 
run in parallel with the project implementation . Integrity and consistency of all dissemination and 
exploitation efforts will be supervised by the Exploitation and Innovation Manager (OSM, see also section 
3.2.1.1b). The strategy for these activities is as follows:  
• Dissemination: all partners will collaborate in dissemination of results among the relevant industry 
sectors;  
• Exploitation: industrial take-up of the materials and related processes developed in DECOAT plays a 
central part: each partner will be involved in defining a preliminary business plan for its key exploitable 
results (KER).  
WP7 includes tasks dedicated to the dissemination and exploitation of the project achievements. 
The Plan for the Exploitation and Dissemination of Results (PEDR) will be completed at the start of the 
project (preliminary version of D7.2, see WP7), improved and evaluated through the duration of the project. 
It will ensure that all project partners have a joint understanding of dissemination and exploitation 
and which knowledge protection measures will have to be taken.   
The draft PEDR  
The draft PEDR presented in this section will be further elaborated from the start of DECOAT and regularly 
assessed and adapted to the actual evolution of the project, see the related (incremental versions of) 
deliverables that will be delivered in WP7. We will also follow and implement as much as possible the 
guidelines as recently published by the EC on social media:  
‘Social media guide for EU funded R&I projects’66.  
A. Target audience and message for a complex network of stakeholders.  
Disseminating project results at working groups and strategic committees beyond the consortium will take 
place. We will address the following groups of stakeholders to maximise the impact: 
plastic/textiles/coatings converters, end users, waste managers, recyclers (to generate a push/pull effect 
across the value chain) but also policy makers and the wide public (to promote the EoL products’ collection 
and promote innovative public procurement for DECOAT solutions).  
B. Channels for dissemination: from public online to interpersonal face-to-face 
interaction.

C. Preliminary overview of dissemination actions: continuous actions and dedicated 
campaigns.  
To disseminate as efficiently as possible (in terms of reach and cost), two main pillars are defined: (i) 
continuous dissemination relying on existing events and means and (ii) organising dedicated DECOAT 
dissemination campaigns and events.   
(i) Continuous dissemination.   
Goal: Raise awareness and interest, ensure that ‘external stakeholders’ remain aware of DECOAT, keep 
them committed and ens ure they can follow the project progress. Creating opportunities for direct 
interaction with interested stakeholders, regular newsletter for sharing project news and announcing 
conferences/events. Continuous activities also include: updating project inform ation on the DECOAT 
website and on social media platforms, distributing leaflets,… Thereby using the materials and channels 
established in DC1 (see below).  
Audience: with this regular stream we want to reach as wide a public as possible, including key 
stakeholders and also the wider public, depending on the content.   
Key message: broadcast of smaller news items, updates, events, … but also more extended updates on 
the progress of the value chain demonstration that will be implemented in DECOAT.  
Organisation: ISWA as WP7 leader will coordinate. Dissemination material will be collected from ALL 
PARTNERS and a schedule will be set-up to efficiently organise the dissemination, leveraging synergies 
and avoiding any possible overlaps.  
(ii) Dedicated DECOAT Dissemination Campaigns (DC)  
Goal: the Dissemination Campaigns (DC) will give a boost to DECOAT’s dissemination efforts at crucial 
times in the project schedule. The goal is not to depend on external events to be able to promote the 
project but to proactively choose our own stages and adequate timing. This requires the ability to garner 
the attention of and create impact with a significant number of relevant stakeholders, and the consortium 
is confident to be able to create this momentum for its camp aigns given the ASG and the associations 
within or closely associated with the consortium and the extensive existing dissemination channels. 
Because the dedicated Dissemination Campaigns (DC) form the key element of the activities, they are 
described separately below.   
DC1 – create awareness. Goal: to create awareness for and stimulate interest in DECOAT based on the 
ambitious goals, innovative nature, commercial and sustainability potential, and the diversity and 
reputation of the consortium members.  Target audience: ASG, materials researchers, potential end-users, 
the coating community, the general public, policy makers, in short: very wide and covering all actual and 
potential target audiences.   
Activities: the dissemination activities will start im mediately through a press release linked to the kick off 
meeting. Further key activities are performed through the setting up of the DECOAT project website and 
having first dissemination material available (e.g. DECOAT leaflet -digital and print), all following a distinct 
project visual identity. Furthermore, the consortium will make extensive use of consortium partners’ 
dedicated professional social media channels, especially LinkedIn and Twitter.  When: by Month 4 - 
Responsible partner: ISWA and CTB (as coordinator)  
DC2 – call for collaboration. Goal: Call for collaboration to continue and expand on DECOAT results that 
need external partners or licensee to be exploited, i.e. results stemming from WP1 -WP4 that can be 
applied outside the use cases of WP5.  Target audience: ASG, innovators/entrepreneurs, large enterprises 
with own R&D, innovative SMEs, related current and future projects, coating/plastic converters, recyclers, 
waste management bodies, product designers and developers.

D. Exploitation activities: identification of results, outline of potential and steps to bring 
to market  
D1 Identification of key results  
The table below (pag. 34) shows the overview of the Key Exploitable Results integrated along 
our partner’s value chain and initial exploitation also in link with previously discussed 
dissemination steps. The results most suited for direct industrial exploitati on are further elaborated 
in part D3 – Steps to market and preliminary business plans below.   
D2 Outlining of the potential market  
Plastic Recycling and recycled plastic market: The global plastic recycling market has been gaining a 
steady momentum over the past few years due to the related directives and growing awareness about 
CO2 emissions and the need to reduce them. This, together with the overflowing state of landfills has also 
triggered an urgent need for recycling plastic instead of producing virg in material. The global plastic 
recycling market, valued at €25 bn in 2015 (of which only €2 Bn in EU67), is expected to reach a figure of 
€45 bn by 2024. During the forecast period of 2016 and 2024, the global market is expected to grow at 
a CAGR of 6.9% . Steady growth is expected in the global market due to ever more stringent laws and 
increasing environmental awareness, especially in EU. In 2012, out of the 25.2Mt of plastics collected for 
recycling, 6.6 Mt were recycled in EU and 8.9 Mt were incinerated. Incineration is massively used, with for 
example 38.8% of plastic packaging waste68. Currently, according to the Plastic Recyclers Europe, 1000 
companies are operating with 30.000 employees and a 3Mt installed capacity. Currently, the European 
installed pl astic recycling capacity is practically being fully used and mostly focused on 
packaging, indicating the huge potential for new investments for and adopting new (more) efficient 
technologies, provided they are proved economical viable.  
We calculate that D ECOAT solutions address ca 3.5 million tons of coated waste material (see section 
2.1.1.3). For calculating our direct impact, we assume that we reach on longer term 8% of the potential 
market, i.e. ca 280ktons. This means that DECOAT can improve the competitiveness and innovation 
capacity for a market segment of ca €1.7billion  (assuming on average a commercial value of ca 
€6000/ton for these coated materials). For reaching a market share of 8%  we count on the market 
pulling effect of the ‘brand owners’ in DECOAT  (Daimler group via MBT, PANASONIC and VAUDE) 
and on the ASG members, especially on the leveraging effect of the industrial associations and networks. 
These will help to motivate companies outside DECOAT to adopt our technology. A natural entry to the 
                                                
67 Coltelli M.B., Aglietto M., Riutilizzo dei materiali polimeri, Edizioni Nuova Cultura, 2015   
68 Plastics Europe, association of plastics manufacturers : Plastics, the facts 2017  
Smart plastic materials with intrinsic recycling properties by design  
Research & Innovation Action addressing CE-NMBP-26-2018  
  
[DECOAT]    Page 41 of 70  
This proposal version was submitted by GUY BUYLE on 28/06/2018 16:55:43 Brussels Local Time. Issued by the Participant Portal Submission Service. 
market will be addressing production waste . Nowadays at industrial level ca. 10-12% of the freshly 
produced coated parts are considered production waste  because of the combination of complex 
coating/painting processes and very high -quality standards, especially in automotive, electric electronic 
and transport industry. These parts cannot be reused because they are ‘contaminated’ with the coating 
material. They can be sold at less than €100/ton for very low added value applications or, even worse, be 
discarded of for land-fill or thermal recovery. This is an important loss factor because the commercial value 
of the parts is estimated at ca. €6000/ton. Estimating the actual material value is diff icult as the coated 
parts concerns both expensive engineered polymers as well as less valuable materials. On average, we 
estimate their value here at €2000/ton. Given the easy material availability (see also above) and an 
estimated recycling cost of ca. €500/ton (for logistics, triggered removal and recycling using CO2 assisted 
extrusion, see also section 1.1.1.1) , DECOAT can offer a viable solution for recuperation of coated 
production waste delivering cost effective secondary materials.  
DECOAT will create a market need for the triggerable additives . Starting again from the 280ktons of 
coated waste material, and assuming that on average a coating or primer layer represents 3% of the total 
part weight and that we need to add 5% of triggerable additives, we calculate a need for 420tons of 
additives per year in Europe. At an average cost of ca. €50/kg, this means a DECOAT triggerable 
additives market of over €20 million annually, or ca. 60 jobs.   
Implementing the DECOAT technology will significantly boost the  recycling sector: if we consider 
again the recycling cost of €500/ton, this corresponds to ca €140million extra annual turnover, or ca 
400 jobs, created by enabling the recycling of these (280kton of) coated parts.   
To realise this, recycling lines will need to integrate (or be updated in case of existing ones) with 
the optical detection technology (estimated at ca €150k/unit by IRIS), the MW technology 
(estimated at €70k/unit by CTECH) and/or the Creasolv technology (estimated at ca. €2000k/unit by 
IVV). Assuming a typical recycling line has a capacity of 5kton/year, approximately 60 lines are required 
to process the target material of 280kton. All lines will need the optical detection system, leading to a 
market of €9million (60 x 150k€). We assume that 30% will use ‘standard ovens’ (no direct DECOAT 
benefit), but 50% of the lines will install MW or other heating equipment, so a market of €2.1million (30 x 
€70k) and roughly 20% a Creasolv system, leading to €24million (12 x €2000k). All this sums up to a one-
time market of ca. €35million , which will create a recurrent annual turnover of ca €7million (20%) 
thanks to related services (consulting, maintenance, updating).

Heating in the research of 
carbonbased materials has 
been mainly limited to liquid 
solutions. Microwave 
heating of solid state 
carbon-based materials and 
especially CNTs, has 
distinct merits, but they have 
scarcely been reported (T. 
Kim, et al, 2014).  
Hybrid CNTs will be 
synthesised either grown on 
SiO2 nanoparticles or they 
will be decorated with metal 
oxide nanoparticles to 
enhance the inductive heat 
due to MW irradiation.  
CNTs are very strong mechanically and 
thermally, recycling should not affect 
their properties. Hybrid CNTs will have 
a competitive advantage in comparison 
to current commercial ones due to their 
multifunctionality. Possible selling price 
€50/kg (as hybrid materials).

the commercial exploitation of the 
hybrid materials. MW equipment: 
licensing to equipment manufacturers,

companies. Expected cost per system

D3 Steps to market and preliminary business plans  
  
The DECOAT industrial partners, whose foreground should ultimately lead to 
industrialisation/commercialisation, have carried out preliminary business plan canvassing from 
which a compound brief is outlined below.  This is based on the market creating potent ial (section 
2.1.1.2), the available amounts of coated waste (section 2.1.1.3) and the outline of the market potential 
(part D2 of the PEDR) and taking into account the improved innovation capacity (see section 2.1.2).  
  
DECOAT BUSINESS CANVAS  
Vision  
Provide sustainable solution 
for introducing  coated 
 parts  into circular 
economy.  
Competitive advantage  
Novel smart polymer material systems for 
debonding of coatings, applicable to the 
main industries - the respective recycling 
method and the related processing.  
Target market  
Coated  materials 
 industries that are 
impossible to recycle up to 
now  
Problem: (multi -layered) 
coated parts not recyclable 
because of permanent 
bonding  
Solution: Create triggered 
debonding systems for 
coated parts, the linked 
recycling process and the 
corresponding circular 
economy roadmap.

materials and processes

booth and presentations

E. Training development  
Audio-visual material will be prepared from the development work and demonstration activities. The 
material will be used to train personnel from the company partners and public material will be put online 
(interactive way) to improve skills and competences of pe rsons working in (mainly) the plastics, textiles 
and recycling industry. Workshops hosted at the pilot plant set ups at AIMPLAS and IVV, among others, 
will be organized to promote DECOAT foreground. The workshops will act as multipliers of dissemination 
efforts.  
F. Standardisation and Regulatory requirements  
Participating in standardisation activities will enable DECOAT to: (i) gain intelligence on standards in areas 
touched by DECOAT, e.g. recycling, sustainability, carbon footprinting, end-of-life and safety; (ii) contribute 
to the development of new standards and ensure that specific needs are taken into account; (iii) establish 
contacts with other stakeholders, experts and regulators at national, European (CEN) and worldwide (ISO) 
levels. See also Task 7.4.  
G. Evaluation of the PEDR  
The plan will also envisage ways of measuring the dissemination and exploitation efforts and its 
impact on DECOAT partners and the stakeholder community . We will continuously pay attention to 
this but also do explicit evaluations before starting the DC2 and DC3 action so that the PEDR can still be 
adapted for the final DECOAT part, see WP7 (Task 7.1).  
Management of research data  
Data and Knowledge produced during the DECOAT project will be handled within the tas ks of the Data 
Management Plan. DECOAT will also join the Open Data Pilot. A data repository will be created in order 
to store raw data, results, reports and deliverables produced during the project. During the early stages of 
the project, partner IRES will discuss with partners and take a decision about the accessibility of the data. 
A justified decision on which simulated and experimental data will be confidential and which will be public 
will be made. As Data Repository, one of the European Open Access is foreseen.   
Raw data generated by instruments during experiments will be open and shared with the public. 
Confidential and sensitive data related with industrial processes or products will be restricted and shared 
only among the Consortium partners. In m atters of reusability, not sensitive or raw data will be shared 
among the research community through existing web Data Repositories such as the UCI repository 70,

In terms of Data Exploitation and Reusability, partners working towards publishing their work (articles, 
monographs, conferences etc., preferably in high impact journals) are committed to comply with the “Open 
Access” guideline of the DECOAT project. More specifically, published work should be easy to access, 
online readable, easy to download and print. This can be done by sharing the above mentioned 
publications into online archives and providing open access to them such as Open Access Infrastructure 
for R esearch in Europe (OpenAIRE) and the Directory of Open Access Repositories (OpenDOAR). 
Knowledge management and IP protection  
Knowledge management to ensure open access. DECOAT project is focused on ‘research & 
innovation’ (this being a RIA) so a fair num ber of scientific articles is expected (see Table pag 32). For 
these publications, publishable results will follow an open-access model in order to optimize the impact of 
publicly funded scientific research, both at European level and at Member State level. For what concerns 
other dissemination activities (Website, Workshops, Conferences and Fairs) DECOAT is fully committed 
to ensure full dissemination and high transparency of the data and results to ensure “public 
access” to project results. Only limitation is that it should be in line with the IPR management . We 
will make for example large parts of the environmental and economic evaluation (from WP6) publicly 
available. The strategy is clearly to provide as much as possible the knowledge generated by DECOAT to 
relevant communities for free, e.g. via partner ISWA and via ASG partners (like EUPC, EuMaT, European 
Coatings) as this will be beneficial for further collaboration and for levering the DECOAT impact.  
IPR Management during DECOAT. Management of knowledge, intellectual property and of other 
innovation related activities involves the recognition of project results/innovations that may have 
commercial value, the protection of novel materials and processes developed in DECOAT and the 
determination of ownership of technologies and resolution of ownership co nflicts. For the success of 
DECOAT all project partners agree on explicit rules concerning IP ownership, access rights to any 
Background and Foreground IP for the execution of the project and the protection of intellectual property 
rights (IPRs) and confid ential information before the project starts. Such issues will be addressed in the 
Consortium Agreement that will be developed in line with the Grant Agreement (cf. H2020 General model 
grant agreement).   
Consortium Agreement. The purpose of the Consortium Agreement is to establish a legal framework for 
DECOAT in order to minimize any internal issues during the DECOAT implementation. It will be based on 
the DESCA 2020 Model Consortium Agreement regulating albeit general definitions and purpose through 
descriptions responsibilities of participating parties, project entry, duration and project termination, as well 
as mutual liabilities.   
Ensuring freedom to operate. For the preparation of the DECOAT proposal a patent analysis was made, 
see section 1.4.2. This  showed that for implementing DECOAT there is freedom -to-operate. During 
DECOAT implementation, the external IP situation will be monitored as part of the exploitation activities 
(see Task 7.2). If forced to by the appearance of an external patent, mitigat ion actions will take place 
leading potentially to an adjustment of the scope of DECOAT.

Access Rights to Background and Results during the project. To ensure a smooth execution of the 
project, the DECOAT partners agree to grant royalty -free Access to Ba ckground and Results IP for the 
project implementation. All project partners will determine any Background IP they are willing to submit to 
DECOAT within the Consortium Agreement before the project starts. IP Ownership within the 
consortium. IP on Results shall be owned by the project partner carrying out the work leading to such IP. 
If any IP is created jointly by at least two partners and it is not possible to distinguish between the 
contributions of each of the project partners, such work will be jointly  owned by them. Any such joint 
inventions and all related patent applications and patents shall be jointly owned by the contributing parties.   
External Non‐Disclosure Agreement (NDA). Any external party interested in testing materials developed 
within DECOAT during the project must write a LOI (Letter Of Intent) to the consortium, indicating the intent 
and purpose of their contribution. Each LOI has to be approved by the consortium and the interested party 
has to sign an NDA.   
(b) Communication activities  
The DECOAT consortium will promote the project and its results, by providing targeted information to 
multiple audiences, in a strategic and effective manner and engaging in one- and two-way exchange. The 
communication activities will start at the onset o f the project and continue throughout its entire duration. 
The communication strategy has the key objective to show the benefits of DECOAT results to the 
European coating, plastics and recycling communities.  
The communication strategy has been delineated to ensure a high visibility of the project and to help 
maximize the impact of results. We follow the guidelines from the Lead Market Initiative Ad -hoc Advisory 
Group for Bio-based Products71: “Design and implement a communication strategy involving all part ners 
in the value chain and all other stakeholders to achieve coherent messages on bio -based products. 
(COM/9)”. Detailed description:  
• Goal: firstly to reach out to the general public to create positive momentum for recycling of materials 
(especially as their collaboration will be needed to collect certain items like EoL textiles) and, secondly, 
make them aware of the impact of EU collaborative projects;  
• Method: using story-telling to make the matter easier accessible and to show the human face of the 
involved people   
• Audience: wider public as coated products are being bought all the time by all the people. As such our 
back pack will be used as an example close to public.  
• Key messages: importance to select recyclable by design products as this will have a life cycle impact.  
• Channels: use of press release in combination with popular media is seen as the most effective to 
reach a larger public. This will be backed up by use of social media (YouTube, Twitter,…) and the 
project website and available public m aterial, accessible for an (interested) layman. These outreach 
activities will be carried out at EU, national, regional and local level, under the responsibility of the local 
partners, but coordinated by the dissemination manager.  
Communication activities  will support the key dissemination moments that are formed by the three 
Dissemination Campaigns (see part C of PEDR) as that should be excellent moments to reach a wider 
audience and to focus on user acceptance in turn generating further demand for recyclable products.   
We will also interact with other European (H2020) projects and relevant European networks, see Task 7.5.

Objectives:   
The main objective in this work package is to develop triggerable materials for debonding on demand.  
• Specification and requirements of end user cases on materials  
• Synthesis of microcapsules, nanoparticles and smart hybrid materials, with triggerable response to 
heat, steam and irradiation, for weakening the adhesive strength and facilitating layer separation  
• Thermo-responsive core-shell nanostructured co-polymers   
• Synthesis of SAP based microcapsules (water or solvent swelling). Synthesis of novel multi-
structured SAPs microcapsules with magnetic nanoparticles to react under MW irradiation.  
• Development of microwave assisted materials (hybrid CNT structures with SiO2 and/or metal 
nanoparticles)  
• Characterization of developed triggerable materials  
  
Description of work  
Within this WP, all triggerable nano - and micro-materials will be developed and characterised, in order to 
work upon specific stimulus (heat, steam, mechanical, microwave, chemical).   
Task 1.1 – Specifications and requirements for end -use cases - Definition of testing procedures - 
CTB, NTUA, CTAG, VAUDE, PANASONIC, MAIER, DAIMLER (M1-M4)  
Within WP1, we will work towards materials that provide a generic solution for de -coating, ie solutions 
generally valid for substrate with a coating of for example PVC, acrylate or TPU. All polymer matrices used 
within the project will be defined. However, to focus the project efforts, we will concentrate on the challenges 
posed by the use cases that are selected by the endusers, see section 2.1.2. Here, we list these parts as 
well as their basic functional requirements. The goal is to further specify these requirements within this task 
so that a clear picture emerges of the core needs (must haves), of the options (nice to have) and of the 
boundary conditions. Moreover, the requirements during operational conditions will be considered, in order 
to choose the appropriate materials, which will be developed in the following tasks.

For example, MAIER started to determine the technical requirements of the final demonstrator parts to

Task 1.2 – Synthesis of microcapsules and nanoparticles with triggerable response to heat and 
steam for weakening the adhesive strength and facilitating layer separation - NTUA, CTB, 
DEVAN, RESCOLL, CTECH (M3-M24)   
In this task, different nano and microstructured materials are proposed for multiple functionalities to 
coatings. One important point that will be considered from the beginning is the compatibility between the 
nanomaterials and coating formulations, that will be tested in WP2. The goal is to make during the first 
phase of the project, adequate quantities of the materials (ca 100g/each) will be made available to WP2 
(coating formulations development & testing) and to WP3 for detection.  
Subtask 1.2.1 – Thermo-responsive core-shell nanostructured co-polymers  
Microstructure particles with a melamine formaldehyde, polyurea or acrylate -based shell comprising 
triggerable response active core ingredients will be developed within this sub-task by DEVAN. The active 
ingredients will be chosen on the basis that upon heat treatment, they will expand and break the 
polymeric microcapsules walls and will be released into the coatings, weakening the adhesive strength 
and facilitate layer separation. In this WP, DEVAN will make use of its know-how (see also section 1.4 
enhance the compatibility of nanomaterials with the coating formulations. At this point, both the chemical 
linkage between the nanomaterials and coating formulations will be addressed, but also the particle size 
distribution of the particles in the coa formulations: homogeneous particles have a less negative impact 
in the coating characteristics, such as gloss loss and smoothness, which will be tested within WP2.  
In this sub -task, different hybrid core -shell na nostructured co -polymers will be also synthesized by 
NTUA. Organic polymers with glass temperature (T g) in the range of 20 oC to 40oC such as poly(propyl 
methacrylate) and poly(butyl methacrylate) will form the core, in order to act as softening points in the 
primers of RESCOLL which will be incorporated. To avoid this function during everyday operation, a 
protective shell will be appl ied around the core. The shell will consist of copolymers such as poly(4 
vinylpyridine-co-methacrylic acid) due to the fact that these polymers demonstrate higher Tg (151oC and 
220o than the processing and operational temperature of the products, as well a s the debonding 
temperature that will be studied in WP2. Furthermore, the presence of methacrylic acid structure units 
in the copolymer shell will impart hydrophilic properties nanostructured co -polymers rendering them 
dispersible in water-based solutions (waterborne formulations). Moreover, the polymers that constitute 
the shell will be cross-linked using an appropriate compound such as ethylene-glycol- 
Furthermore, different dyes which will be investigated and selected, will be used as the initiator for t he 
aforementioned radical polymerizations in order the final product to demonstrate absorption under visible 
irradiation (in-situ polymerisation with the selected dye, in order to enable smart optical sorting in WP3). 
Finally, iron oxides will be incorporated into the shell that will act as magnetic beads into the produced 
core-shell nanostructured co-polymers, which will be under 100 nm. The aim of proposed synthesis is 
the fabrication of multifunctional materials with smart intrinsic sorting properties th at will be able to 
increase their functionality in the presence of a possible magnetic field, due to the presence of inductive 
heat, improving its properties.  
Subtask 1.2.2 –Super Absorbent Polymers (SAP)   
One of the strategies to obtain coatings with debonding properties is to incorporate Super Absorbent 
Polymers (SAP). Cross linked cores will be fabricated via radical polymerization. Furthermore, organic 
dyes will be also used in the structure of the SAPs, to enable the optical sorting in WP3. Different kinds 
of agents can be also incorporated within the SAPs for additional functionalities, to form hybrid materials 
that will react onto stimuli such as microwave irradiation. End-of- 
achieved by exposure to heat sources (e.g. IR radiation), which leads to the melting of the microcapsule 
shell, along with activation of the inner superabsorbent core by steam (moisture), in the case of water 
swelling. The SAPs will also combination with the Creasolv technique (WP2) where they will swell under 
selective solvents to enhance the debonding functionality.

Subtask 1.2.3 – Microwave & Rf assisted materials   
NTUA will investigate the synthesis of hybrid microwave and Rf assisted materials, based on metal 
nanoparticles, silicon carbides and multiwalled carbon nanotubes (MWCNTs). Emphasis will be given to 
the MWCNTs, the growth of which will be achieved through the Catalytic Thermal Chemical Vapor  
Deposition (CT-CVD) method. The MWCNTs will be functionalised with metal nanoparticles or will be grown 
on SiO2 nanoparticles. They will be incorporated in the coating in a concentration lower than 1% wt. The 
aim will be to provide selective heating of th e primer layer and remove the coating, or to melt the coating 
itself and remove the coating layer. Heating effect could be tuned by changing % of additive or to add MW 
transparent materials such as silica or alumina to disperse the particles and moderate the heating effect.  
In addition, in order to enhance the selective heating and melting properties of the adhesive, enhancement 
of the efficiency of induction to microwave will be examined adding nano -sized metal particles with high 
dielectric constant, e.g. zinc oxide. Zinc oxide has high dielectric constant that could shorten the microwave 
treatment time needed for debonding because of the selective heating of the adhesive in a shorter time by 
enhancing the heating efficiency of the adhesive by the irradiation of microwave.  
Task 1.3 –  Triggerable primers for interfacial debonding – RESCOLL (M3-M24)   
RESCOLL will develop debonding primers as described in patent EP1611217. These primers will be based 
on the combination of degradation agents (blowing agents ) and binders, selected to fit with mechanical 
properties and thermal constraints of the different use cases. Thermal triggering of the primers (in the range 
of 100 to 200°C) will allow to cleanly separate the parts at the location of the primers. The nano particles 
and microcapsules developed in Task 1.2 will be incorporated in the primer formulations. The formulated 
primers developed in WP1 will be used as a baseline for further adjustments in WP2.   
Task 1.4 – Selection and up-scaling of the most promising process - DEVAN, NTUA (M12-M39)  
The most promising core/shell microcapsules that will be developed in the Task 1.2 will be selected for 
further up-scaled production. DEVAN will undertake the up -scaling, to provide enough materials for the 
demonstration activities to the rest WPs. The goal is to provide the amounts required for further testing in 
WP2, WP3 and especially WP4 and WP5. Therefore, DEVAN will upscale the most promising prototypes 
in its pilot plant micro-encapsulation reactors with sizes up to five kilograms. DEVAN will check and prepare 
guidelines to scale up the production of the successful microcapsules prototypes to ton scale.  
Task 1.5 – Characterization of developed triggerable materials - NTUA, (M6-M24)  
The aforementioned produced materials (Task 1.2-1.4) will be characterized with several methods in terms 
of their morphology, structure and composition. The average nanocontainer size and their morphology will 
be determined by Scanning Electron Microscopy (SEM) and Transmission Electron Microscopy (TEM). The 
coated and hollow nanocontainers will be characterised by FT -Infrared Spectroscopy (FT -IR), 
Thermogravimetric Analysis (TGA), Differential Thermal Analysis (DTA), energy dispersive xray analysis 
(EDX), dynamic light scattering (DLS) and X -Ray Diffraction (XRD). The magnetic particles to be used in 
thermoplastic matrix will be characterized in this task by means of advanced microscopic techniques.

Smart plastic materials with intrinsic recycling properties by design  
Research & Innovation Action addressing CE-NMBP-26-2018  
  
[DECOAT]    Page 62 of 70  
This proposal version was submitted by GUY BUYLE on 28/06/2018 16:55:43 Brussels Local Time. Issued by the Participant Portal Subm ission Service. 
Description of work  
Task 2.1: Coating formulations, basic coating tests and characterisation - NTUA, CTB, AIMPLAS, 
CTAG, IVV, RESCOLL, DEVAN (M1-M12).   
The triggerable materials developed in WP1 will be added to polymeric coating formulations used in textiles 
(TPU, PVC, acrylates) and in plastics (TPU, PVC, epoxy). The goal is to first obtain coating for mulations 
with a relatively high loading of the triggerable additives and to maintain the required properties of the 
coating formulation, typically viscosity, wettability, foaming properties. Next, also combinations of different 
triggers into a single coating will be tested.  
The main challenge will be to obtain coating formulations with a sufficient loading of the additives, that are 
stable in time, i.e.   the occurrence of agglomeration or sedimentation should be monitored and prevented.   
Next in this task, simple flat A4-samples will be produced on PES textiles and on PC and ABS plastic 
plates. The coating process will be investigated, e.g. the influence of the triggerable additives on drying 
time and wettability.   
At this stage, a single layer of coating will be produced in one thickness. These coated A4-samples will be 
used for physicochemical and mechanical characterisation. This includes chemical and elemental 
composition will be studied by FT -Infrared Spectroscopy (FT-IR) and Energy Dispersive X -Ray Analysis 
(EDX), respectively. The morphology of coatings will be studied with optical microscopy and SEM and AFM 
measurements (to complement the coating surface characterization at nanoscale). The dispersion of the 
nanoparticles and microcapsules in the final solid state coatings will be evaluated with microCT 
measurements. Also other relevant (nano)mechanical properties will be evaluated, e.g. (nano -)scratch 
tests, microhardness, nanoindentation, roughness.   
The produced coated materials will also be used for defining the protocol for measuring the debonding 
effect, see next task.  
Task 2.2: Development of DECOAT protocol for assessment of debonding - CTB, RESCOLL, IVV, 
CTAG, AIMPLAS, NTUA, MAIER, MBT (M3-M18).   
The goal of this task is to define a protocol to determine the debonding or separation capacity of the coatings 
with triggerable additives. Although it might seem fairly easy, this is a crucial step for the project. It will help 
to assess the efficiency of the different options, how they can be compared and optimised.   
This task will start as soon as first results of 2.1 are available, because first triggerable coated substrates 
are needed to debond and to evaluate. We will start from the existing standardised  test ASTM D3330 for 
180° detachment (peel strength test method of adhesive). Goal is to have an objective measurement 
method to be able to determine if the separation is sufficient or not. We will define parameters like the 
residual bond strength, the maximum amount of remaining coating allowed for each material/ end use case 
in order to be able to recycle the substrate material in a high -tech application (circular use). We will also 
develop basic evaluation for measuring the quality of the substrate mater ial after the debonding process 
and for the ability for rebonding/recoating of substrates.   
The outcome of this task will be the ‘DECOAT protocol’ for measurement of the debonding.  
Task 2.3: Optimisation of the coating debonding system and assessment of the separation capacity 
– CTB, AIMPLAS, IVV, NTUA, CTECH, RESCOLL, MAIER (M7-M24).   
Here, we will build on the experience gained in Task 2.1 on the actual coating process. Indeed, we will 
further tune, still using A4scale flat substrates, the concentration of trigger additive, in order to determine 
the lowest concentration needed for an effective separation. The additives will be tested via addition into a 
primer layer (with a trigger -less top layer) and added in a single layer coating. The influence of the 
application method for these layers will be studied, e.g. via direct coating or transfer coating in case of knife 
coating on textiles) as th is results in a different degree of penetration into the substrate. The latter is 
expected to have a large influence on debonding effect. So, the difference between these coating 
techniques will be assessed.  
To structure these activities, one can in princ iple opt for grouping via the triggerable additives or via the 
external trigger applied. Here, we opt to describe as close as possible to how the practical work will be 
organised, which will be a combination of both:   
Smart plastic materials with intrinsic recycling properties by design  
Research & Innovation Action addressing CE-NMBP-26-2018  
  
[DECOAT]    Page 63 of 70  
This proposal version was submitted by GUY BUYLE on 28/06/2018 16:55:43 Brussels Local Time. Issued by the Participant Portal Subm ission Service. 
 To understand the debonding mechanism of the thermally triggered microcapsules their behaviour will 
be investigated by looking at the volume expansion of thermally expansive microcapsules by the 
heating trigger. The shape and size of the particle before and after the heating will be observe d using 
SEM.   
 
Smart plastic materials with intrinsic recycling properties by design  
Research & Innovation Action addressing CE-NMBP-26-2018  
  
[DECOAT]    Page 64 of 70  
This proposal version was submitted by GUY BUYLE on 28/06/2018 16:55:43 Brussels Local Time. Issued by the Participant Portal Subm ission Service. 
• For the coatings triggered by MW, the main activity will be to develop MW processes to ensure sufficient 
uniformity within the cavity. Therefore, several process parameters will have to be tuned: power, pulse 
duration, cavity uniformity of process,… The main challenge will be to determin e the energy input 
needed for good debonding while ensuring none of the materials suffers deterioration, e.g. because of 
charring. MW will be applied to standard susceptors, the novel carbon -based susceptors and SAPs 
functionalised for MW interaction.  
• For the blowing agent based  adhesives and primers  (INDAR technology) we will prepare 
bonded/coated samples for comparative assessment of initial mechanical performance vs reference 
material configurations (i.e. not debondable) and residual mechanical perfo rmance after triggering (in 
the debonded state). Mechanical testing will be based on international standards, depending on 
substrates and applications (peel, lap shear, pull out, scratch test for paints). Thermal ageing resistance 
of the debonding formulations will be tested to ensure that no anticipated loss of mechanical strength 
or debonding capability occurs. The blowing agents will also be combined with MW triggering and with 
microcapsule addition to check reinforcing / synergistic effects.  
• The chemical triggering (the Creasolv process) is based on a difference in dissolution of the coating 
and substrate materials. Different types of coating-substrate combinations will be shredded and fed to 
the process. This will be done for standard coatings and for coatings with SAPs functionalised to interact 
with specific chemical solvents. Further, we will investigate the influence of dense versus foamed 
coatings and the type of coating-substrate interface (from very flat towards high roughness). For each 
material the specific process conditions need to be defined like type of solvents, duration of treatment 
and temperature. Furthermore, specificity of the chosen solvent and dissolution conditions will be 
double-checked with respect to substrate polymers and other plastics types that may be present in real 
waste streams.  After solvent based debonding the purity of the obtained fractions will be evaluated by 
means of e.g. microscopy, IR and DSC.  
The separation capacity of the coating debonding systems based on abov e mentioned various 
combinations of triggerable additives and external triggers will be assessed according to the 
‘DECOAT protocol’ (see Task 2.2). Depending on the additive, the suitable debonding triggers will be 
evaluated: thermal (oven, IR, steam), MW and chemical. For each additive it needs to be checked whether 
the suited debonding process actually leads to a separation of the coating layer and the substrate. It is 
expected that this testing will lead to a further refinement of our testing protocol, so this task will run in strong 
interaction with Task 2.2.   
The measurement protocol will also reveal, after the debonding step, how much residual coating will still be 
present on the textile or plastic. The ‘cleanliness’ of the substrate will be evaluated via microscopy or IR. If 
a considerable amount of impurities is still present, an additional step in the Creasolv process can be 
considered to dissolve these impurities, see also Task 4.3.  
Task 2.4: Broadening the application field of the coating debondi ng system - RESCOLL, DEVAN, 
CTB, CTAG, MAIER, MBT, PANASONIC, VAUDE (M18-M30).   
Up to here all research will be performed on flat substrates of specific chemistry (PES, PC, ABS). Here we 
extend: in real life coated items are not flat, but 3D objects (espe cially for plastics). The coating technique 
will need to be adapted to be able to coat 3D objects. Further, the triggerable coatings will also be applied 
onto standardly used substrate materials like PA for textile and PUR, PE and PP for plastics. The geometry 
and chemistry will have an influence on the separation capacity, so the most promising combinations of 
triggerable material and external trigger will be here assessed again.  
In real life coatings are having a functional performance (see T1.1), which cannot be compromised by the 
addition of the triggers. The developed coating will therefore be assessed for these properties like 
waterproofness, abrasion resistance, weldability (typical for textile substrates) and scratch resistance, gloss 
and anti-corrosion (typical for plastic substrates).  
The outcome of this task will be a portfolio of coating debonding solutions. A solution in the portfolio will be 
specified by the triggerable material, the external trigger, the coating matrix, the substrate material and 
geometry, the need for a primer lay er or not, and an evaluation of separation capacity (energy and time 
needed, cleanliness of substrate).

Task 2.5:  Tailoring of most promised debonding systems for further processing in WP3, WP4 and

According to the results of previous tasks and request, samples for WP3, WP4 and WP5 will be prepared 
when the other WP2 tasks are finished.  
Within tasks 2.2, 2.3 and 2.4, a whole range of materials (combinations) will be developed. Key element will 
thus be the selection and development of solutions ideally suited for the subsequent steps of sorting, (large 
scale) debonding, actual recycling and reuse of the materials. A selection of the most interesting solutions 
will be made based on mechanical properties, processability and performance in the final coated parts (tests 
will be done in the framework of WP5). In addition, focus will be given to the tailoring of the coating properties 
facilitating their further use for the specific end-user parts. A close interaction with WP3, WP4 and WP5 will 
be established to allow constant feedback.

Description of work  
Task 3.1 Monitoring of the microcapsule production and coating and debonding processes - IVV, IRIS, 
NTUA, RESCOLL (M5-M18).   
Within this task, we will develop the optical methods that will help to control the crucial processes of DECOAT: 
microcapsules production (T3.1.1), coating process (T3.1.2) and the debonding process (T3.1.3).  
Throughout this task, samples will be provided  by WP1 and WP2, together with reference measurements 
results, to calibrate the planned photonic techniques.  
Subtask 3.1.1. Microcapsules monitoring via selected dyes  
In line with WP1, IRIS and IVV will work with NTUA to define a range of dyes to be used in the core or shell 
of the microcapsules. Those will be selected in terms of the wavelength they emit, the detection threshold, 
the cost and the compatibility with the coating matrix and substrate material.  
Those dyes will be added during the synthesis process. NIR and measurements based on fluorescence dyes 
(referred to as POLO technique) will be tested to monitor the thickness of the shell (via the ratio with the core 
and size measurement), but also of the crosslinking ratio (as allowed by specific dye s selected for POLO 
system), where applicable. Indeed both parameters are determining the microcapsule response to the 
respective stimuli for debonding.   
Subtask 3.1.2. Primer and nano/micro-composite coating monitoring  
A method based on hyperspectral im aging (HSI) will be developed by IRIS to monitor the thickness and 
distribution of primer on the substrate where it is applied in case of INDAR modified materials to ensure that 
the proper debonding can then take place. In terms of coatings containing eith er microcapsules or metallic 
nanoparticles, a dispersion monitoring system will be set up based on respectively fluorescence spectroscopy 
imaging or diffractometry.  
Subtask 3.1.3. Debonding process monitoring  
The key step for DECOAT is the actual debonding. Within Task 1.1 we will define the requirements for ‘purity’, 
in Task 2.2.2 we will work out a protocol for determining, off -line, the debonding capacity. Here, we will 
develop the optical method to monitor the debonding process online.   
Samples with various degrees of removed coatings will be provided to assess the capability to quantify 
residual coating material. This is needed as such contaminations caused by residual coating material can 
affect the further reprocessing. Hence, the optical 
 
monitoring can provide the necessary quality control. This will be based on POLO for materials containing the 
fluorescent dyes or on HSI.  
  
Trigger to be monitored  Possible technique  Parameters  
Thermo-responsive core shell nanostructured co -polymers eg. Core poly(propyl POLO, NIR Shell Crosslinking 
methacrylate) or poly(butyl methacrylate) + DYE 1, Shell:  poly(4-vinylpyridine-co- Core/shell Ratio methacrylic acid), 
), Crosslinked shell (using e.g. ethylene-glycol-bismethacrylate), optionally with nanoparticles (NPs) + DYE 2 (POLO)  
Super Absorbent Polymers (Core: Crosslinked poly(methacrylate-co-ethylene glycol POLO, NIR Core Crosslinking 
dimethacrylate) + DYE 3 (POLO) +  Shell: organic polymers such as poly(ethyl Core/shell Ratio methacrylate) and 
poly(methyl methacrylate) + DYE 4  
Microwave & Rf assisted materials (MWCNTs, MWCNTs functionalised with  NIR,

nanoparticles (e.g. ZnO, iron oxide silica or alumina) + optional DYE 5

spectroscopy HSI

Note: The above dyes used in the core shell structure are for the trigger material detection within final items so that the 
right treatment can be selected for disassembly. Further dyes will be considered to integrate in the core of the 
microcapsules to track for example the risk of premature triggering.   
Task 3.2 Selection of the identification techniques for triggerable materials - IRIS, IVV, RESCOLL, 
DEVAN, NTUA (M9M24).   
This task deals with the identification in a waste flow of the parts that include the triggered materials developed 
in the project. Test will begin with single-point measurement before shifting to imaging solutions that can deal 
with macro -scale objects. It is relevant to automatized waste sorting facilities but also as the triggered 
materials may only be locally discernible in section views (e.g. in case of primer/adhesives as opposed to 
coatings, especially for bonded parts, level 1 disassembly) or in case a spatial distribution of 
thickness/residual coating is needed. Tests will init ially start with monomaterials of triggered materials and 
then continue with coated substrates (case that will be validated in WP4-5) and bonded materials.  
For the microcapsules dispersed in a coating or adhesive, we will rely on detecting the dyes that were selected 
in the previous task independently of the matrix polymer to which they are added. Related inputs from IVV 
obtained during task 3.1 will be provided as a basis. Fluorescence spectroscopy coupled with an HSI camera 
will be used as most versatile technique but alternative techniques could be considered in case of insufficient 
resolution or for post project exploitation in more specific cases to lower the inv estment such as: (i) 
Fluorescence spectroscopy single point in tandem with a conventional (unfiltered) machine vision camera; (ii) 
Filters and grey-scale camera with a set of narrow band-pass filters in tandem with a conventional (unfiltered) 
machine vision camera; (iii) RGB camera (only if dyes emit in the Visible range and can be distinguished).  
For the INDAR primer or MW triggerable materials nanocomposites , we will respectively use NIR based 
technique and fluorescence spectroscopy (optionally X ray or simple fluorescence in case also fluorescence 
dyes are grafted to the particles). The techniques will be calibrated with a relevant set of samples from WP2. 
For each case where several options are possible, the most suitable one will be selected depending on cost, 
detection level, versatility (e.g. to detect several of our triggers), etc.  
Task 3.3 Sorting prototype concept and prototyping - IRIS, IVV (M19-M39).   
Subtask 3.3.1. Design and building of the hardware systems  
Based on the results of the offlin e trials performed in the previous task and in interaction with the design of 
the pilot line from WP4 (Task 4.2), the architecture of the system hardware will be conceptualized. The needs 
(and linked suitable positioning) for the inspection systems will be  defined. In principle we need the initial 
detection and post coating removal verification. That will be the basic hardware system. Two variations will 
also be considered: the option without the monitoring of the removal and the option to identify triggera ble 
parts before the assembly/grinding.   
The optical systems, as well as the enclosure and other optomechanical parts will be designed using 
mechanical CAD tools (e.g. SolidWorks) with inputs from ray -tracing software (e.g. ZEMAX) or Maxwell 
solvers when appropriate. The components (light source, beam shaping and imaging optics, detector and 
data acquisition hardware) will be selected from commercially available options whenever possible and be 
customised when necessary.   
Subtask 3.3.2 Software development  
The software is needed for driving the instruments, processing the data by means of predictive models and 
delivering the results

through the user interface and towards the pilot line operators. Hardware drivers and the raw data acquisition 
layer will be set up for the core device used in the previous subtask. The right modelling package will be

Subtask 3.3.3 Hard- and software validation

validated before transferring and integrating the system into the pilot line. The hardware, software and 
calibration models will be adjusted as needed. A user manual including operational gui delines will be 
delivered. Factory Acceptance Test (FAT) procedure will be implemented following ISO 9001:2008 
processes.  
Task 3.4 Guidelines for use of optical techniques for monitoring and quality control in coatings and 
textiles and plastics processing – OSM, IRIS, IVV (M28-M39).   
Within tasks T3.1, T3.2 and T3.3 several techniques will be tested and applied to plastics and textiles 
processing. Indeed, we will develop solutions for the process control of the microcapsules production, of 
coating thickness and uniformity, of the debon ding. We will also determine the level of contamination (from 
the left-over coating) on plastics and textiles as well as the presence of specific markers within waste streams.  
These results will be used to provide guidelines for required process monitoring systems to be implemented 
in line during the post project upscaling phase to support constant production quality. However, these results 
will be also be useful beyond the applications foreseen within DECOAT. Therefore, we will also compose 
guidelines for use next to DECOAT.
